Preface
This MasterтАЩs Thesis represents a partial fulfillment of the requirements for a Master of
Science degree in Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The research
project was conducted under the guidance of Associate Professor Philip Skov Halding at
DTU. The project comprises 30 ECTS points and took place from January 22, 2024, to
June 22, 2024.
The author would like to express his sincerest gratitude to his supervisor, Philip Skov
Halding, for his enthusiasm, shared knowledge, support, and guidance throughout the
entire project period, making it a pleasure to work on.
Rihards Zamoidiks
June, 2024
i
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Arch structures have been built since ancient times and are considered optimal for their
ability to resist dead and uniformly distributed loads through pure compression. How-
ever, high costs, challenging construction, and labor intensity make them less desirable
compared to other alternatives. Pearl-Chain technology, developed at the Technical Uni-
versity of Denmark, aims to reintroduce arch structures through innovative application
of precast concrete and post-tensioning technology, minimizing the drawbacks of conven-
tional arch structures. When post-tensioning force is applied, a series of precast concrete
elements are pressed together, resulting in an integrated structure that can then be lifted
into its designated location. Additionally, applying the principle of sandwich structures
to closed-spandrel arch bridges enables composite action, significantly increasing load
bearing capacity and improving material efficiency.
The primary objective of this thesis was to build on previous research, which focused
on the use of SL-Deck Pearl-Chain arches, and to explore the applicability of cassette
elements for further improvement in material efficiency in bridge construction.
Through analytical calculations and modelling, optimal configurations for the arch and a
cassette type Pearl-Chain element were identified. A comparison of equivalent elements
showed that the cassette design results in a significant reduction of CO2 emissions com-
pared to the SL-Deck counterpart. These results were further supported by the calculated
global warming potential of a complete sandwich arch bridge, showcasing approximately
14-16% overall reduction compared to an equivalent SL-Deck sandwich arch design. These
findings highlight the potential for improvement in sustainability in bridge construction
through the application of Pearl-Chain technology with cassette elements.

==PAGE:19==
1 Introduction
1
Introduction
1.1
Background
Arch structures have been built since the ancient times, with some examples of Babylonian
masonry arches and Chinese and Roman stone arches that are still in use today [1].
In general, arch structures are considered optimal due to their ability to resist dead
and uniformly distributed loads through pure compression. However, they are also well
known for their drawbacks, including the challenging and costly construction process and
large thrust forces at the supports, especially with increasing span lengths [2]. While
efficient for uniformly distributed loads, the critical load conditions typically arise from
non-symmetrically positioned loads [3], resulting in higher demands on the load bearing
structure and thus less optimal material usage.
Nowadays, in the construction industry, there is a strong collaborative effort in minimizing
global emissions, making the reduction of material usage a top priority. Consequently,
significant efforts have been made at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) to
reintroduce arch bridges through the concept of sandwich structures, leveraging advanced
concrete technologies such as Pearl-Chain (PC) and post-tensioning (PT) reinforcement
technologies.
The idea of using the deck of an arch bridge to increase the bearing capacity of the entire
structure is not entirely new. In the early 1920s, Swiss bridge engineer Robert Maillart
pioneered the design of deck-stiffened arch bridges. Unlike traditional hollow-box girder
designs, MaillartтАЩs design utilized transverse cross-walls, ensuring an integral connection
between the deck and the arch. This connection helped to minimize bending moments in
the arch, allowing it to be much thinner and lighter. His first deck-stiffened design was
the Flienglibach bridge that was constructed in 1923 (Fig. 1.1 (a)) [4].
(a)
(b)
Figure 1.1 | (a) Flienglibach bridge (1923) [5] and (b) Karl P. BillnerтАЩs arch bridge concept
[6]
Furthermore, a patent filed in 1933 by Karl P. Billner [6] described an innovative approach
to closed-spandrel arch bridges (Fig. 1.1 (b)), utilizing lightweight concrete filling material
and ensuring composite action between the deck and arch - that today is known as the
sandwich arch bridge concept.
In 2007, DTU Professor Kristian D. Hertz invented light-weight load bearing structures,
closely followed by the development of the PC technology. This innovation lead to the
establishment of a startup company called Abeo Ltd. Through further development, Abeo
1
==PAGE:20==
1 Introduction
introduced the Super Light Deck (SL-Deck), a lightweight and material efficient solution
for constructing multilevel buildings, while also maintaining design considerations for PC
structures [7, 8].
The PC principle enabled the construction of material efficient curved structures without
the need for complex scaffolding and the associated labor intensity. Further study on
this technology was conducted by Philip Skov Halding, now an Associate Professor at
DTU, who, as a part of his PhD research, investigated the application of PC technology
for closed-spandrel arch bridges.
His research ultimately led to a construction of the
first ever PC Bridge, known as the Vorgod Creek Road Bridge [3]. This bridge project
utilized pervious concrete for backfill material, which has a porous structure and excellent
water-draining properties, and has been tested for use in PC arch bridges [9].
This development initiated a series of Master thesis projects at DTU, during which differ-
ent students investigated the potential of utilizing the strength and stiffness of the filling
layer and a concrete top plate to increase the load bearing capacity of the bridge through
the principle of a sandwich structure.
In 2015, Nanna Tange Bech and Maria Holst
Pedersen conducted research titled "Development and Test of Arch Bridge with Materials
of Different Stiffness", during which small-scale model testing confirmed that the total
load bearing capacity of a sandwich arch bridge can be significantly increased compared
to a plain PC arch structure [10]. In 2017, Andreas Benjamin Elbr├╕nd performed struc-
tural analysis on two sandwich arch bridge concepts: one using a regular closed-spandrel
three-hinge setup and another using transverse wall stiffened arch [11]. In 2021, Synne
L├╕vold analyzed the structural response of sandwich arch bridges subjected to live traffic
loads [12], while Fredrik Hole Flaa and Sondre Tjetland conducted a comprehensive study
on the structural analysis and optimization of sandwich arch bridges utilizing SL-Deck as
PC elements and assessed their competitiveness against other common bridge types [13].
Outside of DTU research, the Re:Crete project demonstrated the potential for PC tech-
nology in material reuse by constructing a 10 meter span PC arch footbridge using cut-up,
reclaimed precast concrete elements [14].
1.2
Scope
The primary objective of this thesis is to optimize PC elements to further improve ma-
terial efficiency in bridge construction. Building on the foundation of previous research,
this study explores the applicability of cassette PC structures in combination with the
sandwich principle for bridges.
As an introductory part of this project, a literature study on sandwich structures was
conducted during a special course in January 2024. Some of the findings are presented in
this report, while the complete report is available in Appendix A.
The hypothesis of this project is formulated as follows:
The substitution of SL-Deck PC elements with cassette type concrete elements
offers a more material efficient solution for sandwich arch bridge design.
2
==PAGE:21==
1 Introduction
Consequently, the following research objectives are outlined:
1. Perform analytical calculations to determine the optimal parameters for a closed-
spandrel arch bridge configuration.
2. Analyze concrete cassette elements to identify configuration that maximizes load
bearing capacity while minimizing material usage.
3. Identify the applicable load cases and combinations for a sandwich arch bridge,
considering the specifics of PC and closed-spandrel arch bridge construction.
4. Analyze PC arch sandwich bridges of various spans utilizing cassette elements.
5. Compare material efficiency and sustainability of SL-Deck and cassette PC arch
sandwich bridge designs.
Conversely, the following parts are considered to be outside the scope of this study:
1. Environmental loads, including wind, snow, and temperature induced loads.
2. Accidental loads, such as vehicle impact and earthquake loads.
3. Dead loads related to the self-weight of road surface layers, bridge safety equipment.
4. Abutment design to resist the horizontal support forces.
5. Serviceability Limit State verification for deflection control, evaluating both indi-
vidual cassette elements and the designed PC arch structure as a whole.
6. Detailed reinforcement design for the cassette element.
1.3
Thesis guide
The following points serve as a guide for the reader, presenting a brief overview of each
chapter and its contents:
тАв Chapter 2 - Presents the relevant theory on arch structures, PC technology, sand-
wich structures, and calculation basis for structural analysis.
тАв Chapter 3 - Summarizes the investigation of the optimal arch configuration for
closed-spandrel arch bridges, including analytical results and discussion.
тАв Chapter 4 - Introduces precast concrete cassette elements and the theory for load
bearing capacity calculations. It investigates the influence of various parameters to
identify the optimal configuration, presenting analytical results and a comparison
between the cassette and SL-Deck PC elements.
тАв Chapter 5 - Provides an overview of structural analysis for PC arch sandwich
bridges, identifying the applicable load cases and load combinations, followed by
Ultimate and Serviceability Limit State verification.
It concludes with a design
comparison of different span lengths and the calculation of the resulting Global
Warming Potential (GWP), comparing the results against the SL-Deck designs.
3
==PAGE:22==
2 Theory
2
Theory
2.1
Arch structures
In architecture and civil engineering, an arch is defined as a vertically curved and axially
compressed structural member used to span openings and support loads from above [15].
Arch structures are advantageous due to their load carrying mechanism, where uniform
loads are primarily carried in compression, or in the case of a true arch by pure compres-
sion. In a true arch, dead loads mainly produce normal forces, while non-uniform and
live loads will also result in bending moments [7, 16]. With compression being the pri-
mary force acting on the cross-section, a variety of materials, including timber, masonry,
concrete, metal, and composites can be used in their construction. Concrete, due to its
favorable relation between its production cost and compressive strength, is particularly
suitable for application in arch structures [1, 15].
Figure 2.1 | Load transfer in an arch structure
Due to the geometry of an arch and the way it carries load, externally applied loads
produce both vertical and horizontal support reactions (Fig. 2.1). While a disadvantage
for abutment design, in arch structures, the horizontal forces reduce bending moments
across the entire span, resulting in generally lower values compared to an equal span
beam [12, 16]. As a result, arches are typically used only where horizontal forces can
be effectively transferred to the foundation.
Introducing a tie, a member connecting
both ends of the arch (Fig. 2.6 (b)), also known as a bow-string (or tie rod), can relieve
foundations of the horizontal component of the arch thrust force, however, such practice
considerably limits the structureтАЩs applicability [1].
2.1.1
Arch classification
The structural response of an arch primarily depends on the load conditions, geometry
and its static system. The overall shape of the arch is defined by its span length, rise
height and centerline shape, while the static system is determined by boundary conditions,
such as support and hinge arrangement. Consequently, arch structures are categorized
based on their shape and static system.
4
==PAGE:23==
2 Theory
2.1.1.1
According to centerline shape
Historically, arch structures have utilized various centerline shapes, including circular
(semicircular, segmental or compound circular), elliptical, (second-order) parabolic, (in-
verse) catenary and in some cases even polygonal [15]. As previously established, a true
arch, one that closely follows its thrust line under permanent loads, can be an economical
and sustainable structure in terms of material consumption. However, non-uniform or live
loading on top will cause the thrust line to deviate from the archтАЩs centerline, generating
bending moment [1]. When a vertical uniformly distributed load is applied across the en-
tire span of an arch, the optimal shape is a parabola. However, if the arch is only bearing
its own weight, then an inverse catenary, also known as the chain line, is better suited to
achieve the state of a true arch, theoretically resulting in no bending moment across the
span. When considering both types of loads simultaneously, the optimal solution could
be a circular shape тАУ a close average of the two [7]. A visual comparison of the circular,
parabolic, and catenary curves with rise-to-span ratios of 1/4 and 1/10 are presented in
Fig. 2.2.
Figure 2.2 | Arch centerline shape comparison for circular, parabola and catenary curves
For a specific span length and rise height, the arch shape can be described by using the
selected shape functions described by Eq. 2.1, 2.3 and 2.4, where y(x) is the arch centerline
height, x is the distance along the span, h is the rise height at the crown and L represents
the span length. Additionally, Eq. 2.2 is used to calculate the radius of a circle and Eq. 2.5
is used to solve for the parameter a, that describes the distance to the lowest point above
the horizontal axis in a regular catenary curve [13, 17].
Circular curve:

As observed from Fig. 2.2, the distinction between the different curves becomes more
noticeable at higher rise-to-span ratios. However, at lower values where the arch flattens,
all three curves appear to converge. This indicates that for shallow arches, the exact
formulation of the arch curve becomes less significant. Consequently, a circular curve
becomes preferable in a segmental construction approach, enabling the prefabrication of
identical building elements [3, 7].
The rise of an arch, and thus the rise-to-span ratio, can be influenced by various local
conditions such as the geometry of the obstacle the arch is spanning, clearance require-
ments, or foundation conditions. It is an important parameter in arch configuration for
both structural and aesthetic considerations. Generally, neither very low nor very high
rise-to-span ratios are desirable from a combined perspective of aesthetics, economics,
and practical implementation. Regardless of the specific arch shape and its characteristic
bending moment distribution, the rise-to-span ratio largely determines the magnitude of
the horizontal reactions at the supports [1]. A smaller ratio or a flatter arch will result
in relatively larger compressive forces. While advantageous for arch structures made of
stone or concrete, due to the stabilizing effect of pre-compression [3], it also leads to
considerably larger horizontal support reactions, potentially requiring more massive and
costly abutments, depending on the foundation conditions. Conversely, an arch with a
high ratio results in smaller horizontal reactions but generates larger bending moments
due to an increase in arch length and self-weight of the structure [15].
Figure 2.3 | Major arch bridges; comparison of rise-to-span ratios [1] (for the sake of conti-
nuity, the original image has been edited to present rise/span ratio, instead of span/rise ratio)
6
==PAGE:25==
2 Theory
Typically, the rise-to-span ratio falls within the range of 1/2 to 1/10, with the most
common cases ranging from 1/3 to 1/6. For shallow arches, a minimum ratio of 1/20 has
been proposed to maintain arch behavior, ensuring efficient design by primarily resisting
loads through compression rather than bending [15]. Fig. 2.3 presents the rise-to-span
ratios of several major arch bridges across the world.
2.1.1.2
According to static system
According to the static system, arch structures are typically classified into three-hinged,
two-hinged and fixed arches, as illustrated in Fig. 2.4. In a three-hinged arch, there is
one hinge at each support and one at the crown, allowing for rotations at each location
and resulting in a statically determinate structure. Conversely, a two-hinged setup only
has hinges at the supports, while a fixed arch has no hinges altogether, making them
statically indeterminate to the first and third degree, respectively [1, 15].
Figure 2.4 | Arch classification by static system: a) three-hinged, b) two-hinged and c) fixed
arch [15]
Arch structures are sensitive to secondary effects, such as arch shortening due to shrinkage
or creep, elastic deformation, temperature variations or yielding of supports, all leading to
a change in the arch shape. These effects can lead to considerable changes in the resulting
forces, but the degree of arch sensitivity largely depends on factors such as rise-to-span
ratio and static indeterminacy. As the rise-to-span ratio or the number of hinges increases,
the sensitivity to secondary effects tends to decrease [1, 15].
While the three-hinged system is the least sensitive to secondary effects, from a statics
point of view, it is generally less desirable due to its lack of reserve strength. In the event
of support yielding, there is an increase in the distance between the supports, resulting
in a reduction of the rise height. Consequently, this leads to an increase in the horizontal
reaction forces, creating a self-reinforcing mechanism that, in worst cases, can lead to a
collapse. This is particularly crucial for shallow arches, as the lower the rise-to-span ratio,
the greater this effect becomes [1]. Furthermore, having three hinges in an arch system
means that the structure is only one plastic hinge away from becoming geometrically
unstable, again leading to a collapse.
Conversely, a two-hinged system exhibits considerable reserve strength.
In this case,
support yielding is resisted by an increased positive bending moment, resulting in reduced
horizontal support reactions and effectively countering this tendency [1, 3].
Alternatively, a fixed arch can improve the overall stiffness of the structure and provide a
more even distribution of bending moment. However, it has the drawback of increased sen-
sitivity to secondary effects. For example, compared to a two-hinged setup, a fixed arch is
7
==PAGE:26==
2 Theory
less capable of accommodating uneven support settlements, resulting in stress concentra-
tions near the abutments, while a two-hinged setup allows for hinge rotation, mitigating
these effects [3]. Furthermore, in addition to the arch thrust, a fixed arch also introduces
reaction moment at the supports, further increasing the structural requirements for the
abutment design [18].
2.1.2
Arch bridges
An arch bridge is a type of bridge that uses an arch as its primary load carrying system.
Like other arch structures, arch bridges can be categorized by their static system, shape,
and construction materials. Additionally, they may be further classified according to the
spandrel structure and the relative positions of the deck and the arch rib [15]. The typical
terminology used to describe arch bridges and their components is depicted in Fig. 2.5.
Furthermore, arch bridges may be designed as single span or multi-span bridges, consisting
of a series of arches with identical or varying span lengths, adjusted to the height of the
bridge deck above the ground [1].
Figure 2.5 | Arch bridge terminology
According to the placement of the deck, arch bridges are categorized into deck arch,
through arch and half-through arch bridges, as illustrated in Fig. 2.6. A deck arch bridge
has its deck positioned above the crown of the arch (Fig. 2.6 (a)), which is a typical
configuration for a true arch bridge and the most common type of arch bridge in general.
In contrast, a through arch bridge has the deck placed at the springing line of the arch
(Fig. 2.6 (b)), with its horizontal support reactions typically carried by a tie rod or a
girder connecting the two ends, resulting in a tied arch system, as introduced earlier. In
a through arch configuration the loads from the deck are transferred to the arch through
tension hangers.
Lastly, if the bridge deck is placed at an elevation above the springing line, but below the
crown, it is referred to as a half-through arch bridge (Fig. 2.6 (c)) [2, 16].
8
==PAGE:27==
2 Theory
Figure 2.6 | Classification of arch bridges according to the relative positions of the deck and
arch rib: (a) deck arch bridge, (b) through arch bridge and (c) half-through arch bridge [15]
In the case of deck arch bridges, the space between the deck and the arch is called spandrel.
If this space is filled with soil or another type of filling material, the traffic load from the
deck is transferred through this filling layer onto the extrados of the arch, resulting in
what is called a closed or filled spandrel arch bridge (Fig. 2.5 - left). Conversely, if there
are openings in this space, the structure is referred to as an open spandrel arch bridge
(Fig. 2.5 - right). In open spandrel arch bridges, the loads from the deck are transferred
through spandrel columns or a truss-like bracing systems, in which case the structure
would be classified as a braced sprandrel or truss arch bridge [1, 15].
Arch bridges can offer several advantages. Because of their load carrying mechanism,
they allow for more efficient material utilization and, as a result, have a large span ca-
pacity. Additionally, they are relatively rigid and experience minimal deformations in
service state, ensuring better driving conditions compared to other large span capable
bridge types, such as cable stayed or suspension bridges, and typically require less overall
maintenance [16].
2.1.3
Structural response
The structural behavior of an arch structure depends on the specific shape and static
system. A true arch is only subjected to compressive forces in its cross-section without
any bending moment. This is the case for specific arch shapes subjected to particular
load distributions, such as a three-hinged circular arch under a radial uniform load, a
parabolic arch under a vertical uniform load, and a catenary arch under a varying load,
proportional to the axis curve elevation. However, achieving a true arch in applications,
such as an arch bridge, is practically impossible due to various types of loading applied,
most notably moving live loads [15].
For arch structures, uniform loading across the span is rarely the critical load condition,
as they are designed to accommodate such load distributions. The critical conditions
typically involve non-symmetrically positioned loads, such as heavy point loads moving
across the span. The most critical positioning of such loads would be at the quarter point
of the span for a point load or covering one half of the span for a uniformly distributed
load (UDL) [3, 7].
As outlined in Section 2.1.1, a three-hinged arch features a hinge at each support location
and at the crown. This configuration makes it a statically determinate system, allowing
for static analysis through simple equilibrium equations. Conversely, e.g., a two-hinged
arch does not have a hinge at the crown, therefore it is statically indeterminate to a single
degree. Consequently, determining support reactions for such a system requires a more
complex approach, such as the displacement or unit-load methods.
9
==PAGE:28==
2 Theory

support reaction, initially substituted by a unit load, M1 is the resulting bending moment
from the said unit load, Mp is the bending moment from external loads, and L is the span
length of the arch [18].

2.2
Pearl-Chain technology
The conventional method for constructing arches or any other curved concrete structures
involves complex temporary scaffolding systems and curved moulds along with significant
labor for installation. This, being a major drawback in a comparison to other structural
systems, has resulted in arch structures becoming less attractive [7].
In 2007, DTU Professor Kristian D. Hertz invented light-weight load bearing structures,
closely followed by the development of the PC technology. This technology is based on
innovative application of PT systems and precast concrete elements incorporating pre-
installed cable ducts. When PT force is applied, a series of precast concrete elements are
pressed against each other, generating compressive forces at their interfaces and resulting
in an integrated structure that can then be lifted to its designated location. All without
the need of a complex scaffolding system. While the PC technology is primarily applied
to arch structures, the specific shape of a PC structure can be adjusted based on the
geometry of the concrete elements and the cable profile within. This principle is illustrated
in Fig. 2.8. As long as the cable profile is perpendicular to the PC joints at their location,
the joints are subjected to pure compression [7, 8].
Figure 2.8 | Pearl-Chain principle
11
==PAGE:30==
2 Theory
If the PC structure takes the form of an arch, the pre-tensioning of precast concrete ele-
ments, post-tensioning, and the dead load all contribute to compressive forces within the
arch cross-section. This has an overall stabilizing effect, as any bending moment arising
from external loading and the resulting tensile stresses may be resisted through unloaded
compression. Additionally, this results in reduced cracking of the concrete elements and
the joints between them [7].
Fig. 2.9 illustrates the construction of the first ever PC arch bridge, known as the Vorgod
Creek Road Bridge (Denmark), where a fully assembled SL-Deck PC arch is being lifted
into its designated location [7].
Figure 2.9 | Construction of the Vorgod Creek Road Bridge, Denmark [7].
2.3
SL-Deck
The SL-Deck is a prestressed concrete slab that combines lightweight aggregate concrete
(LAC) with a compressive strength of 3 MPa and a density of 800 kg/m3, along with
self-compacting concrete (SCC) with a compressive strength of 55 MPa and a density of
2400 kg/m3. The LAC is cast with a specially curved geometry, creating LAC blocks,
as detailed in 2.11, which leave both longitudinal and transverse grooves. These grooves
shape the geometry of the stronger SSC structure, resulting in a ribbed slab surrounded
by an insulating and stabilizing layer of LAC. Additionally, the SL-Deck is reinforced with
pre-tensioning strands, placed in the longitudinal grooves of the slab and additional mild
reinforcement transversely, ensuring the establishment of cross-reinforcement. A typical
SL-Deck panel, excluding the reinforcement, is illustrated in Fig. 2.10 [8].
Figure 2.10 | SL-Deck by Abeo [8]
12
==PAGE:31==
2 Theory
The SL-Deck offers a range of benefits, but what sets it apart from other more conven-
tional precast slab systems is its high flexibility in geometry, improved sound insulating
properties, and a minimum of four hours of fire resistance. This makes it the most fire-
resistant precast slab system in the world, without the need for additional fire-insulating
materials. Both the sound insulating and fire-resistant properties are largely attributed
to the use of LAC [8, 19].
2.3.1
SL-Deck Pearl-Chain elements
As stated earlier, the SL-Deck has been thoroughly studied for its application in PC arches.
The larger gap between the central LAC blocks, as seen in Fig. 2.11, has been designed
intentionally to accommodate a PT cable and its curvature [7]. Additionally, numerous
pre-tensioning strands are supplied for both transportation purposes and increasing the
level of pre-compression, thus improving both positive and negative bending moment
resistances. The example in Fig. 2.11 showcases a cross-section of an SL-Deck adapted
for use in PC structures, similar to the one used in the Vorgod Creek Road Bridge [20].
Figure 2.11 | Cross-section of an SL-Deck used in PC structures [20]
2.4
Sandwich structures
The following parts of this subsection provide a partial recap on the findings from a
literature study on sandwich structures. As stated in Section 1.2, this study was conducted
by the author during a special course in January 2024 as an introductory part for this
project. The complete review is available in Appendix A.
Sandwich panels are multilayered structures consisting of one or more high-strength and
stiffness layers called faces (or skins, wythes), bonded to one or more flexible layers with
considerably lower density, called the core.
As illustrated in Fig. 2.12, the faces are
typically made from identical materials and thickness. This configuration, similar to an
I-beam, results in increased strength and stiffness without a substantial increase in weight.
In this analogy, the faces act as flanges, primarily carrying load in tension/compression,
while the core, similar to a sectionтАЩs web, provides structural support, increases bending
and torsional stiffness and carries the majority of the shear load [21].
13
==PAGE:32==
2 Theory
Figure 2.12 | Illustration of a common sandwich panel geometry [22].
2.4.1
Sandwich structure classification
Sandwich structures are categorized according to their layer composition and core as-
sembly type. According to the layer composition, three categories can be identified: an
ordinary sandwich, consisting of two outer faces surrounding a single lightweight core (as
presented in Fig. 2.12), an open-face sandwich or semi-sandwich, consisting of only one
face and a core, and a multi-layer sandwich which consists of multiple cores and facings.
Concepts for all three panel types are illustrated in Fig. 2.13. Among these, ordinary
sandwich panels are most commonly used [21].
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 2.13 | (a) Ordinary [23], (b) Open-face and (c) multi-layer sandwich concepts [24].
In terms of core assembly, generally, two groups are distinguished: homogeneous and
structured core materials.
Homogeneous materials are further divided into solid and
foam cores, while structured core materials are categorized into honeycomb, corrugated,
and textile cores [21].
2.4.2
Composite action
To fully extract the potential of a sandwich structure, a high degree of composite ac-
tion between its components is required. The degree of composite action in structural
elements can be classified into fully composite, partially composite and non-composite.
This classification is based on the shear flow capacity determined by the bond and shear
connector strength between core and face layers [21].
14
==PAGE:33==
2 Theory
Figure 2.14 | Strain distribution for different degrees of composite action [25]
.
A section is considered fully composite when all of the longitudinal shear, as illustrated
by the linear strain profile in Fig. 2.14 (a), is transferred between the faces, resulting in
all components of the system working together as a unit in resisting bending moment. On
the other hand, if there is no shear transfer, the section is non-composite, still allowing the
components to share load but bend independently around their neutral axes, as illustrated
by Fig. 2.14 (c). Partial composite action falls somewhere between the two extremes,
involving only a fraction of shear transfer between the faces (Fig. 2.14 (b)). The schematic
in Fig. 2.15 further illustrates the influence of the different degrees of composite action
on layer interaction and the resulting deflections [21].
Figure 2.15 | The influence of degree of composite action on load transfer and deflection [21]
A higher degree of composite action not only increases the load bearing capacity, but also
improves the structural behavior predictability, enabling more efficient design through
analytical calculations. However, achieving full composite action in sandwich structures
is rather challenging, meaning that a partial composite action is more common, leading
to a structural behavior that is more difficult to predict [21]. This aspect constitutes a
significant research topic on its own, therefore, for this study a full composite action is
assumed, focusing specifically on the optimization of PC elements.
15
==PAGE:34==
2 Theory
2.4.3
Sandwich arch bridges
A sandwich arch bridge is a special type of a closed-spandrel arch bridge that utilizes the
sandwich concept. Typically, in closed-spandrel arch bridges, the primary load bearing
structure, the arch, is constructed from either in-situ cast or precast concrete elements
[3].
As the name suggests, the space between the arch and the roadway is typically
filled with loose soil such as dirt or gravel, or lightweight concrete, on top of which
the pavement, typically asphalt, is laid. This conventional arch bridge composition is
illustrated schematically in Fig. 2.16 (a). As depicted in the figure, the vertically applied
load is distributed among the different layers, however, each of the components are working
independently. This configuration results in a non-composite action, which is sub-optimal
in terms of the achievable load bearing capacity and material efficiency.
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.16 | (a) Conventional and (b) Sandwich arch bridge working principles
On the other side, Fig. 2.16 (b) illustrates the working principle of a sandwich arch
bridge. Such a system can utilize the same arch structure as the conventional design,
or incorporate the PC technology, as outlined in Section 2.2. Additionally, it includes a
concrete top plate and a filling layer capable of transferring shear between the upper and
lower surfaces. If sufficient bond between the layers and shear capacity of the backfill
layer are ensured, the bridge section becomes composite and resists loads similar to a
sandwich structure. In this system, the arch and the top plate work together to resist
external loads through a compression and tension force couple [7, 13]. Consequently, the
load bearing capacity of a sandwich arch bridge section primarily relies on the dimensions
of the arch and top plate cross-sections, as well as the vertical distance between the two,
which varies along the span.
2.5
Calculation basis for structural analysis
In Denmark, structural design is primarily governed by the Eurocode - a set of technical
standards that provide a common approach to the structural design of buildings and other
civil engineering works [26]. Additionally, each country has its own National Annexes,
derived from national standardization documents, which define parameters left open in
the Eurocode for national choice. For this project, the relevant Eurocode (EC) parts,
including National Annexes (DK NA), are listed in Table 2.1 [27].

2.5.1
Dead loads
Dead loads are permanent structural loads that remain constant throughout the entire
service life of a structure, including the self-weight of the structural members and all other
materials comprising the structure. For this study, the considered dead loads are:
1. Self-weight of the PC arch
2. Self-weight of the backfill material
3. Self-weight of the top plate
Due to the specific construction method of PC arch bridges, involving arch lifting and
backfill pouring, different dead load distributions are considered to identify the critical
load case, yielding the highest internal forces within the structure.
2.5.2
Live loads (traffic loads)
In bridge design, the primary live loads to consider are the traffic loads, representing the
vertical loads of the cars, lorries and specialized vehicles. According to the Danish Road
Directorate, bridge Group I includes all bridges for public roads designed to accommodate
normal traffic conditions, with the capacity to also transfer heavy specialized vehicles
[27]. EC1, Part 2, Section 6.3 [28] defines the applicable live loads for bridges and their
distribution on the carriageway, where traffic loads are categorized in load models (LM).
Out of the four load models, LM2 and LM4 are deemed irrelevant for this study, as LM2
covers dynamic effects on shorter structural members (3 - 7 meters), while LM4 covers
crowd loading, intended for general verification [28]. Therefore, only LM1 and LM3 are
considered. Environmental loads, including wind, snow, and temperature induced loads,
as well as accidental loads such as vehicle impact, are also not covered in this study.
17
==PAGE:36==
2 Theory
2.5.2.1
Load Model 1
Load Model 1 (LM1) represents traffic loads from lorries and cars and is applicable for
general and local verification. It consists of two partial systems: the tandem system (TS)
for double axle concentrated loads and the uniformly distributed load (UDL) system, as
depicted by Fig. 2.17.
Figure 2.17 | Load Model 1 according to EC1, Part 2, Section 6.3.2 [28]

2.5.3
Limit state design
2.5.3.1
Ultimate limit state
Ultimate limit state (ULS) calculations are required for the purpose of ensuring the struc-
tural integrity and safety under extreme conditions. For this study, the relevant ultimate
limit states, as covered by EC2, Part 2, Section 6 [30], include the calculation of:
тАв Compressive normal force resistance
тАв Bending resistance with or without normal force
тАв Shear resistance
2.5.3.2
Serviceability limit state
Serviceability limit state (SLS) covers design verification for normal use conditions. As
outlined in EC2, Part 2, Section 7 [30] this includes:
тАв Stress limitation under characteristic loads
тАв Crack control
тАв Deflection control
In concrete members, both compressive and tensile stresses that exceed their critical
values can result in crack formation, leading to a reduction in durability. According to
EC2, Part 2, Section 7.2 [30], in the absence of measures such as increased concrete cover
or confining transverse reinforcement, compressive stresses should be limited to k1 ┬╖ fck,
where k1 takes the recommended value of 0.6 [30]. Meanwhile, tensile stresses should be
limited to the mean tensile strength of concrete fctm. Complying with these limitations,
as per EC2, Part 1-1, 7.1(2) and EC2, Part 2, 7.2(102) [30, 31], allows the cross-section
to be assumed as uncracked.
19
==PAGE:38==
2 Theory
As PC elements are individual components of a larger structure, their deflection control
is not addressed, while the overall deflection of the arch is assumed to comply with the
requirements as it falls outside the scope of this study.
2.5.3.3
Load combinations
Load combinations involve simultaneous application of different load cases to simulate
critical load conditions for dimensioning purposes. The applicable load combinations for
bridge structures, for both ULS and SLS, are obtained from the Design Guide for Load
and Calculation Basis for Bridges [27] and are based on EC0 DK NA [32]. Additionally,
for ULS the consequence class load factor KFI is applied to all unfavorable structural
loads. In the case of bridge structures, a consequence class of CC3 is applicable, defining
KFI = 1.1, as specified in EC0, Table A1.2 DK NA [32].
For ULS and SLS verification, the considered load combinations include the load factors
listed in Table 2.2 and 2.2, respectively. Furthermore, LM3 axle loads are modified by
the same load factor as the TS axle loads in LM1.

2.5.5
Prestressing
Technical specifications for both pre-tensioning and post-tensioning systems are sourced
from the European Technical Approvals (ETA) provided by CCL [33, 34], a supplier of
prestressing systems and installation equipment. PC structures require the use of bonded
PT cables, comprised of bare, unsheathed 7-wire strands placed within corrugated metal
ducts. Following the application of PT loads, the ducts are filled with cement grout to
ensure optimal bond and force transfer to the structure. In this study, post-tensioning
strands with a nominal diameter of p.pt = 15.7 mm and a corresponding cross-sectional
area of Ap.pt.n = 150 mm2 are considered. This consideration defines various PT system
specific parameters such as anchorage, duct, and concrete cover dimensions. Similarly,
pre-tensioning strands with a nominal diameter of p.pre = 12.5 mm and a cross-sectional
area of Ap.pre.n = 93 mm2 are selected. Steel strands with an ultimate tensile strength of
1860 MPa and a relaxation Class 2, according to EC2, Part 1-1, Section 3.3.2 [31], are
considered for both systems.
21
==PAGE:40==
3 Optimal arch configuration
3
Optimal arch configuration
To optimize a PC arch sandwich bridge, it is important to analyze not only the PC
elements, but also take a holistic approach to the entire bridge system. For arches, as
outlined in Section 2.1, the structural response is primarily defined by their static system
and shape, while for closed-spandrel arch bridges, the backfill height and its material
properties also have a considerable effect on the overall response.
In this section, calculations and key takeaways from the analysis on optimal design pa-
rameters for the arch structure in a closed-spandrel arch bridge are described. A more
detailed overview of this analysis can be found in Appendix B.
3.1
Dead load from the backfill material
In a closed-spandrel arch bridge, the space between the top surface and the arch is filled
with a backfill material. Therefore, while investigating the structural behavior of dif-
ferent arch configurations, it is important to accurately define the expected dead load
distribution.
The characteristic dead load of the backfill layer can be calculated using Eq. 3.1, where
╬│pc = 19 kN/m3 represents the specific weight of the pervious concrete backfill material
[9], W is the width of the arch element, and hbf(x) describes the backfill layer thickness
along the span and is calculated according to Eq. 3.2. Here, y(x) represents the arch shape
function, and hbf.s is the backfill height measured from the support level. For simplicity,
only the self-weight of the backfill material is considered, and an arch width of 1 meter is
applied for all calculations.
gbf.k(x) = ╬│pc ┬╖ W ┬╖ hbf(x)
(3.1)
hbf(x) = hbf.s тИТy(x)
(3.2)
3.2
Static system
As discussed earlier, arch structures typically incorporate between one to three hinges in
their structural system. The greater the number of hinges, the less sensitive the structure
is to secondary effects such as arch shortening due to shrinkage or creep, elastic or tem-
perature induced deformations, or foundation settlements. Additionally, the static system
itself has a significant influence on the archтАЩs structural response to external loads.
To determine the influence of the selected static system on the structural response of
the arch, a static analysis was conducted considering dead load conditions outlined in
Section 3.1 with a backfill height of 0 m above the crown and an arch with a circular
shape, a rise-to-span ratio of 1/10 and a span length of L = 20 m. The resulting bending
moment distributions between three-hinged (3H), two-hinged (2H) and fixed (0H) arch
configurations are depicted in Fig. 3.1, while results for other internal forces are available
in Appendix B.
22
==PAGE:41==
3 Optimal arch configuration
Figure 3.1 | Bending moment distribution for different static systems under the dead load
of the backfill material
From the results depicted in Fig. 3.1, it is observed that, due to the hinge placement
at the crown, the three-hinged system experiences no negative moments, consequently
resisting external loads through relatively large positive moments. In contrast, similar
to a continuous beam, the two-hinged setup introduces negative moments in the middle
section of the arch, resulting in a more even moment distribution throughout. Lastly, the
fixed arch, in alignment with the theory described in Section 2.1.1, exhibits the lowest
bending moments across the majority of the span but results in a peak negative moment
at the supports.
Fig. 3.2 illustrates the relationship between the maximum and minimum bending moment
values and horizontal support reactions according to the static system. The fixed arch
exhibits the lowest horizontal support reactions, but results in a peak negative moment at
the same location, potentially placing even higher requirements on the abutment design
compared to a system with a larger horizontal reaction but no moment reaction.
In
contrast, the two-hinged system, despite having a larger horizontal reaction force, offers
the advantage of a more even distribution of bending moments.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.2 | (a) Max and (b) Min bending moment - horizontal reaction
With the considerations outlined in Section 2.1.1 and the observations listed above, it is
concluded that a two-hinged configuration generally offers an optimal solution in terms of
23
==PAGE:42==
3 Optimal arch configuration
structural response and safety. It provides considerable reserve strength, lower sensitivity
to secondary effects, a more even distribution of bending moments, resisting more of
the load through compression, and reduced structural requirements on abutment design.
Furthermore, given the specifics of PC arch and closed-spandrel arch bridge construction,
the two-hinged setup allows for easier implementation.
3.3
Centerline shape
The most common centerline shapes for arch structures are circular, parabolic, and cate-
nary curves. In Fig. 2.2, the differences between these shapes at various rise-to-span ratios
were illustrated, concluding that for shallow arches, any of the definitions may be se-
lected due to near converging results between all three. Conversely, at higher rise-to-span
ratios, the differences are more notable, leading to greater differentiation in structural
response. Under a uniformly distributed load, this variation primarily arises from the
slight differences in the moment lever arm for the horizontal support reactions, while for
a closed-spandrel arch bridge, the different shapes also alter the backfill distribution.
To quantify these differences, a static analysis was conducted considering a two-hinged
static system with dead load conditions outlined in Section 3.1 with a backfill height of
0 m above the crown, rise-to-span ratios of 1/10 and 1/4, a span length of L = 20 m and
three centerline shapes: circular, parabolic, and catenary. The results for the bending
moment distribution are presented in Fig. 3.3, while other internal force distributions are
covered in Appendix B.
Figure 3.3 | Bending moment distribution for different centerline shapes and rise-to-span
ratios (1/10 and 1/4) under backfill dead load, analyzed for a two-hinged static system
The results in Fig. 3.3 show that the structural response for shallow arches, i.e. low rise-
to-span ratio, shows nearly converging bending moment distributions across all centerline
shapes, as is the case with the shape functions themselves. This tendency is further sup-
ported by Fig. 3.4, for the relationship between the maximum and minimum bending
moment values and horizontal support reactions. At rise-to-span ratio of 1/10, the vari-
ation between the peak moment values is near negligible, with differences of 6.5% and
2.8% for the positive and negative moments, respectively.
The difference in structural response is more apparent for the rise-to-span ratio of 1/4,
where the parabolic shape exhibits the largest bending moment values, closely followed
24
==PAGE:43==
3 Optimal arch configuration
by the catenary curve. Under the given dead load distribution of the pervious concrete,
the circular shape appears to be the most effective both in terms of bending moment
distribution and the resulting horizontal support reactions. Upon closer inspection, this
difference is partially attributed to the difference in the total dead load of the backfill
and its distribution along the span. For a rise-to-span ratio of 1/4, the difference in total
backfill material between the parabolic and circular shapes is approximately 2.3 m3 per
meter width, accounting for additional 43.7 kN in dead load.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.4 | (a) Max and (b) Min bending moment - horizontal reaction for different cen-
terline shapes and rise-to-span ratios (1/10 and 1/4) under backfill dead load, analyzed for a
two-hinged static system
Based on these findings, the circular shape is found to be superior in both structural
response and material efficiency for use in closed-spandrel arch bridges. Furthermore, as
stated earlier, the circular shape also enables the prefabrication of equal concrete elements
for PC arches.
3.4
Rise-to-span ratio
As covered in Section 2.1 from both structural and aesthetic considerations neither a very
low nor very high rise-to-span ratio for an arch structure is desirable. Typically, the rise-
to-span ratio falls within the range of 1/2 to 1/10, with the most common cases ranging
from 1/3 to 1/6. The specific ratio determines the magnitude of horizontal reactions
at the supports, compressive forces in the arch section and most importantly bending
moment distribution along the structural member.
To investigate the influence of this parameter, a static analysis was conducted considering
a two-hinged system with a circular centerline shape and a range of rise-to-span ratios,
namely 1/14, 1/10, 1/6, 1/4, 1/3 and 1/2. As before, a span length of L = 20 m and dead
load conditions as outlined in Section 3.1 with a backfill height of 0 m above the crown,
were applied. The results for the obtained bending moment distributions are presented
in Fig. 3.5, while other internal force distributions are available in Appendix B.
25
==PAGE:44==
3 Optimal arch configuration
Figure 3.5 | Bending moment distributions for different rise-to-span ratios under the dead
load of backfill, analyzed for a two-hinged static system with a circular centerline shape
From Fig. 3.5, a clear tendency is observed - for shallow arches, the lower the rise-to-
span ratio, the lower the peak bending moment values for both positive and negative
moments. However, beyond a ratio of 1/4, a shift in structural behavior is noted. At
a ratio of 1/3 the peak values for positive moments decrease compared to those at 1/4,
while the negative moment at the middle of the span remains relatively constant. Further
increase in rise-to-span ratio leads to a considerable difference in the overall moment
distribution, characterized by large negative moments near the supports and a reduction
of both positive and negative moments in the middle region of the span. This observation
is explained by the fact that an arch with a rise-to-span ratio of 1/2 effectively forms
a perfect semi-circle. Consequently, nearing a ratio of such magnitude, there is a rapid
initial increase in the bending moment lever arm for the horizontal support reaction,
overpowering the counteracting effects from the vertical reactions.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.6 | (a) Max and (b) Min bending moment - horizontal reaction for different rise-
to-span ratios under backfill dead load, analyzed for a two-hinged static system and a circular
centerline shape
As before, Fig. 3.6 presents the horizontal support reactions for various rise-to-span ratios
in a relation to the peak positive and negative bending moments. The shift in structural
behavior described above is also evident here, occurring for rise-to-span ratio increase
26
==PAGE:45==
3 Optimal arch configuration
somewhere beyond 1/6, but most notably 1/4, where the trend of increasing peak values
for both positive and negative bending moments with the increase in rise-to-span ratio is
reversed. An exception to this occurs at the ratio of 1/2, where a significant increase in
negative bending moment near the supports is observed.
For the rise-to-span ratio, the optimal choice is not as straightforward as with the previous
parameters, as there appears to be a range of viable values between 1/14 and 1/6. This
range ensures the lowest and most uniform moment distributions across the span, given
the considered dead load conditions. This is observed for shallow arches due to higher
thrust forces, reducing the magnitude of the bending moment throughout. However, as
previously stated, this also presents a considerable challenge in abutment design due to
increased structural requirements.
The results in Fig. 3.6 suggest that there is design flexibility to accommodate poorer
soil conditions by increasing the rise-to-span ratio, thus reducing arch thrust force but
compensating by an increase in bending moment. Conversely, for rocky foundations, a
full advantage of a shallow arch may be utilized, resulting in a more material efficient
arch structure.
On the other hand, Table 3.1 presents the backfill material volume per meter width of
the arch, based on the design considerations outlined above. It is observed that towards
the higher end of the previously stated viable range of rise-to-span ratios, there is a
significant increase in material consumption, compared to the lower end of it. This means
that the potential material savings from a lighter abutment construction may be offset
by an increase in backfill material instead.
Rise-to-span ratio [-]
1/14
1/10
1/6
1/4
1/3
1/2

Table 3.1 | Backfill material volume per meter width of the arch for various rise-to-span
ratios, a circular centerline shape and a backfill height of 0m above the crown
Based on these observations and aiming for improved material efficiency, while maintain-
ing some design flexibility, it is recommended to select the rise-to-span ratio within the
range of 1/14 and 1/10, which is well within the recommended values that maintain arch
behavior.
3.5
Backfill height
Lastly, the effect of additional backfill material was considered. Previously, all calcula-
tions assumed backfill height equal to the level of the crown. However, as discussed in
Section 2.1, additional dead load contributes to pre-compression of the arch, which has
a stabilizing effect and allows for greater bending moment resistance through unloaded
compression.
Therefore, to assess the effects of additional backfill, a static analysis was conducted
considering the minimum and maximum values of the previously determined optimal
27
==PAGE:46==
3 Optimal arch configuration
range of rise-to-span ratios (1/14 and 1/10), a two-hinged arch with a circular centerline
shape, a span length of L = 20 m and dead load conditions as outlined in Section 3.1 with
a varying backfill height of 0, 1, and 2 m above the crown. The results for the bending
moment distributions are presented in Fig. 3.7, with further details on other internal
forces available in Appendix B.
Figure 3.7 | Bending moment distributions for rise-to-span ratios of 1/14 and 1/10 under
varying height backfill dead load, analyzed for a two-hinged static system with a circular cen-
terline shape
The results show that, irrespective of the rise-to-span ratio, an increase in backfill height
leads to a reduction in bending moment across the span. As mentioned earlier, this is
primarily due to increased arch thrust forces caused by the additional dead load of the
backfill. With an increase in backfill height, the arch resists more of the external load
through compression compared to bending.
Between the two considered rise-to-span ratios, the effects of increased horizontal support
reactions and decreased bending moments are more distinct for the higher rise-to-span
ratio with each step of additional material height.
Interestingly, it is found that the
bending moment distribution for a rise-to-span ratio of 1/14 with a backfill height of 0 m
above the crown nearly aligns with the results for 1/10 with a 2 m backfill height. This
highlights how much more material efficient the arch structure can be if the foundation
is able to resist the horizontal support reactions.
In a sandwich arch bridge, the amount of backfill material above the crown is primarily
determined by the sandwich action - the required shear force resistance of the backfill layer
and the normal force resistances of the arch and top plate components. Overall, the results
show that additional material has a beneficial effect on increasing arch pre-compression
and reducing bending moment across the span.
28
==PAGE:47==
4 Optimal Pearl-Chain element configuration
4
Optimal Pearl-Chain element configuration
Previous research has successfully investigated the application of SL-Deck elements for
PC arches. As discussed in Section 2.3, the unique design of an SL-Deck offers numerous
advantages, however, not all of these are necessarily beneficial in bridge structures. For
example, the addition of LAC blocks, among other benefits, improve sound insulating
properties of the deck, which, while not quite as beneficial for bridges, comes at the cost
of increased material consumption.
Although SL-Deck has proven to be a viable option for PC arch construction, a study
by Flaa and Tjetland, 2021 [13] concluded that the resulting GWP of an SL-Deck arch
sandwich bridge becomes lower compared to a typical concrete girder bridge only for
span lengths above 25 meters, as highlighted by the estimation depicted in Fig. 4.1.
This can potentially be explained by the design constraints characteristic of the SL-Deck
geometry, resulting in lower material utilization at shorter spans. Additionally, the LAC
has significantly lower compressive strength compared to the regular concrete used in the
rest of the deck. Therefore, depending on the exact configuration, under high compressive
stresses, the SL-DeckтАЩs negative moment capacity may be decreased significantly [3]. This
is a critical consideration for any arch structure other than those utilizing a three-hinged
static system.
The objective of this section is to analyze the structural behavior of a new type of PC
element - a cassette, which is investigated as a potential improvement for use in PC arches.
Figure 4.1 | Material GWP of SL-Deck PC arch sandwich and girder bridges [13]
4.1
Cassette elements
A cassette element is a structural element that consists of a concrete frame and a slab part
of lower height than the frame itself, as illustrated in Fig. 4.2 [7]. It can be compared to
a ribbed structure, such as a double T-beam or, in this case, even an open-face sandwich
panel, as introduced in Section 2.4. Similar to the SL-Deck, this type of element offers
the possibility of being mass produced in a factory setting.
29
==PAGE:48==
4 Optimal Pearl-Chain element configuration
Figure 4.2 | Cassette element
From the perspective of PC arches, a cassette element has four side walls (ribs) that can
serve as joint surfaces to neighboring elements, both longitudinally and transversely. By
ensuring that the longitudinal ribs have sufficient thickness and height to accommodate
a PT cable and its curvature, it enables the possibility of forming a PC arch, as demon-
strated in Fig. 4.3. Depending on the total width of the structure, similar approach may
be applied transversely through either stressing bars or additional PT cables to fix the
adjacent PC arches together. This can facilitate load sharing, increasing the load bearing
capacity of the structure.
Figure 4.3 | Cassette Pearl-Chain arch structure
In sandwich arch bridges, orienting the cassette element as shown in Fig. 4.3 improves
shear transfer between the backfill layer and the PC arch, which is critical for achieving
a high degree of composite action. Alternatively, flipping it upside down may result in
additional material savings, provided that the bay section of the cassette remains unfilled.
However, the benefit of improved shear transfer arguably outweighs the potential savings
in backfill material. As covered in Section 2.4, shear transfer capacity in sandwich struc-
tures is often the limiting factor in achieving optimal composite action and, consequently,
a more material efficient solution.
30
==PAGE:49==
4 Optimal Pearl-Chain element configuration
4.2
Ultimate Limit State calculations
In this section, the methods for calculating the design resistances of the cassette section for
normal force NRd, positive bending moment MRd.pos, negative bending moment MRd.neg,
and shear force QRd are described. For this example and all further calculations, the
cassette element is assumed to be oriented as illustrated in Fig. 4.4. This figure also
provides a more detailed overview of the overall composition of the section, including
some of the relevant terminology.
Figure 4.4 | Cross-section (A-A) and longitudinal section (B-B) of an example cassette ele-
ment
A cassette element has varying sectional properties along its longitudinal axis, requiring
analysis of four critical sections, as depicted in Fig. 4.5:
тАв Section of the concrete wall just before the mortar joint, with the PT cable at the
sectionтАЩs neutral axis (NA) (WALL)
тАв Section right after the concrete wall, where the reduced concrete cross-section begins
and the PT cable is at its lowest position (END)
тАв Section at the middle of the element, where the PT cable is at its peak rise (MID)
тАв The mortar joint itself (JOINT)
At section WALL, the PT cable should always be positioned at the NA of the section
and aligned at a right angle with respect to the end inclination. Such an arrangement
ensures a smooth cable profile, reducing friction losses and preventing additional bending
moment formation at the joints.
Generally, each section should be analyzed perpendicular to the central axis of the joined
structure тАУ in this case, the PT cable profile, which follows the arch axis. However, in
this case, because a low rise-to-span ratio arch is considered, the sections are analyzed
31
==PAGE:50==
4 Optimal Pearl-Chain element configuration
perpendicular to the elementтАЩs longitudinal axis. This approach yields conservative results
due to a slight reduction in the overall cross-section area. An exception to this is the
mortar joint, which is analyzed according to the end inclination, as indicated in Fig. 4.5.
Figure 4.5 | Critical cross-sections of a cassette element in a PC arch
The following parts of this section describe the method for determining the load bearing
capacities of a cassette element in ULS, and generalized formulas applicable to all critical
sections in Fig. 4.5 are presented. This method is compiled into a parametric calculation
template and is used in further analyses. A detailed overview of this template is available
in Appendix C.
4.2.1

and geometry and are therefore calculated for each of the critical sections outlined above.

4.2 are introduced for both the cassette and mortar joint sections, based on the material
properties listed in Table 2.4.

4.2.2.3
Negative bending moment resistance
The negative bending moment resistance MRd.neg is calculated following the same method
as for the positive moment resistance, with the main difference being that the compression
block is now at the bottom of the section, as shown in Fig. 4.9, which illustrates the

4.3
Cassette element optimization
To investigate the impact of various section parameters on the positive and negative
moment resistances and identify the optimal configuration for a cassette element, an
analysis is conducted following the method outlined in Section 4.2. This analysis covers
several cases: A - rib height, B - rib width, C - slab height, D - slab width, and E/F -
cross-sectional areas of top and bottom pre-tensioning strands, respectively, and case G -
cross-sectional area of post-tensioning strands. Each case monitors the change in resulting
moment resistances due to a gradual increase in the dimension of the specific parameter.
For this analysis, a baseline configuration depicted in Fig. 4.10 is considered, correspond-
ing to the critical section END, with a concrete strength class of C55/67, Ap.pt = 600 mm2
(4 strands), positioned at the centroid of the wall section, Ap.pre.top = 558 mm2 (6 strands),
and Ap.pre.bot = 558 mm2 (6 strands), applying the prestressing forces to their design ca-
pacity. Additionally, this analysis considers no additional normal forces NEd acting on
the section besides the prestressing.
A detailed overview of the calculations in this section can be viewed in Appendix D.
Figure 4.10 | Baseline configuration for cassette section parameter influence analysis
39
==PAGE:58==
4 Optimal Pearl-Chain element configuration
For each parameter concerning section geometry, the dimension increment corresponds to
a 5% increase in the total concrete cross-sectional area, while for prestressing steel, each
increment corresponds to four additional strands for top and bottom pre-tensioning and
two additional strands for post-tensioning. The results of this analysis are depicted in
Fig. 4.11 and 4.12, while Fig. 4.13 describes the resulting positive/negative moment ratio
across these parameters.

From the results in Fig. 4.11, as anticipated, rib height Hrib shows the most significant
influence on both positive and negative moment resistances among the sectionтАЩs geomet-
rical parameters. Additionally, rib width Wrib has a considerable effect on the positive
moment resistance, but has no impact on the negative counterpart. The slab dimensions,
Hslab and Wslab, show no effect on positive moment resistance, while slab width shows
practically negligible improvement for the negative moment resistance.
On the other hand, results in Fig. 4.12 indicate that varying the quantity of prestressing
steel yields a more significant increase in both positive and negative moment resistances
compared to increasing the concrete cross-sectional area. Additionally, Fig. 4.13 shows
that rib width Wrib and bottom pre-tensioning reinforcement Ap.pre.bot have the largest
influence on balancing the moment resistance ratios, with rib height Hrib following closely.
Conversely, additional post-tensioning and top pre-tensioning reinforcement, Ap.pt and
Ap.pre.top, respectively, lead to larger negative moment resistances compared to the positive.
4.3.1
Cable rise
As previously covered, the PT cable follows the exact curvature of the arch centerline.
This results in a varying eccentricity along the cassetteтАЩs longitudinal axis, with the cable
ends positioned at the centroid of the section. Consequently, the cable rise depends on the
span length, rise-to-span ratio, arch shape function, and the length of the individual PC
elements. Assuming a circular arch shape, the peak rise of the cable within an element
can be determined by calculating the saggitta of a circle as described by Eq. 4.44, where
R is the arch radius, and Lel represents the PC element length.
ypt.peak = R тИТ
s
R2 тИТ
Lel

(4.44)
In practice, to achieve the desired cable profile, the PT duct must be placed to accom-
modate the eccentricity of the strand bundle in a tensioned state. This eccentricity is
determined according to the size of the duct and the number of strands, both of which
are defined by the selected anchorage system.
In a PC arch, once PT forces are ap-
plied, the strand bundle is pressed against the lower side of the duct. This is illustrated
in Fig. 4.14 for different cable sizes, according to CCL ETA-07/0035, considering the
nominal strand diameter of 15.7 mm [33].
Figure 4.14 | Post-Tensioning strand eccentricity in a duct for different cable sizes

according to Eq. 4.45, where Hel is the PC element height, cmin.pt is the minimum concrete

of span lengths for a circular arch with a rise-to-span ratio of 1/14 and PC elements of
varying lengths. The figure also presents the upper limit for cable rise according to the
section height, assuming a 7-strand PT cable (XM-30).
It is observed that for a shallow arch and shorter span lengths, the section height may
limit the length of an individual PC element to achieve the desired arch geometry. Fur-
thermore, excessively long PC elements will result in a significant increase in cable rise,
generating larger positive bending moments from PT application alone, therefore reducing
the effective positive moment resistance.

4.4
Section comparison
To assess whether the cassette design offers a more material efficient solution for PC
arches compared to an SL-Deck approach, a direct comparison is conducted. Using the
method covered in Section 4.2 and findings from Section 4.3, intuitive adjustments are
made to the cassette configuration to achieve a section that, under no additional normal
force NEd, yields the same positive and negative bending moment resistance as its SL-
Deck counterpart. For this comparison, the SL-Deck section is chosen similar to the one
used for the Vorgod Creek Road Bridge [3], with both the SL-Deck and cassette sections
illustrated in Fig. 4.16.
Since both sections exhibit larger moment resistances in one
direction compared to the other, the cassette element is flipped along its longitudinal axis
to ensure directly comparable results.
Both elements are analyzed using concrete of the same strength class C55/67 and an equal
amount of prestressing steel, Ap.pre.tot = 1116 mm2 and Ap.pt = 900 mm2.
42
==PAGE:61==
4 Optimal Pearl-Chain element configuration
Figure 4.16 | Cross-section comparison: SL-deck (left) and cassette element (right)
Additionally, the change in bending moment resistances arising from the application of
additional normal force NEd is analyzed for both sections. The structural behavior of the
SL-Deck is assessed using a calculation template provided by DTU, adapted for use with
PT reinforcement in PC arches, and is available for overview in Appendix E. The results
of this analysis are presented in Fig. 4.17 and 4.18.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.17 | (a) Positive and (b) negative moment resistances for SL-Deck and cassette
The results in Fig. 4.17 reveal that the cassette section exhibits a continuously increasing
negative bending moment resistance with the application of additional normal force, while
the SL-Deck experiences nearly instantaneous decline. As mentioned earlier, this behavior
is attributed to the low compressive strength of the SL-DeckтАЩs LAC blocks. Consequently,
the moment resistance ratio for the SL-Deck exhibits a rapid increase, as depicted in
Fig. 4.18, while the cassette shows a more steady trend throughout. Conversely, the SL-
Deck section shows a more significant increase in positive moment resistance, while the
cassette peaks at around 4000 kN, nearing the limit of its normal force resistance NRd.
Furthermore, data points labeled "cassette flipped" indicate that when the panel is in its
intended orientation, as described by Fig. 4.3, its behavior aligns more closely with the
SL-Deck design.

==PAGE:62==

Figure 4.18 | Positive/negative moment ratio comparison for SL-Deck and cassette

==PAGE:63==
4 Optimal Pearl-Chain element configuration
The data presented in Fig. 4.19 showcase the potential of the cassette approach to PC
arch design, offering approximately a 26% reduction in GWP compared to an equivalent
SL-Deck element.
Furthermore, it indicates that concrete constitutes the majority of
the GWP in a PC arch, especially when high-strength concrete is required, whereas the
GWP associated with prestressing steel is only a small fraction of the total. Considering
this, along with the conclusions from Section 4.3, it is evident that the cassette element
should incorporate an optimal amount of prestressing reinforcement to maximize material
efficiency.
It is important to note that the specific configuration of the cassette section, shown in
Fig. 4.17, is not necessarily fully optimized for use in PC arches but is directly comparable
to the original SL-Deck design, starting with nearly identical bending moment resistances
at no additional normal forces. Adjustments based on the conclusions from Section 4.3
should lead to even further improvements in material efficiency.
Further analysis of the projected material savings in a fully assembled PC arch sandwich
bridge, with cassette elements configured for optimal material utilization, is presented in
Section 5.

5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
5
Sandwich arch bridge analysis
To investigate the performance of the cassette PC element in a complete bridge structure,
preliminary design calculations are conducted for a PC arch sandwich bridge of various
span lengths. Certain design parameters and assumptions are chosen to ensure direct
comparability with the previous study of SL-Deck PC arches [13]. However, it is ensured
that these choices align with the optimal configuration limits identified in Section 3.
This section provides an overview of a calculated example for a bridge with a span length
of 27.0 meters, with more detailed calculations and the results for other spans available
in Appendix F.
5.1
Bridge configuration
Four different span lengths are considered with a fixed rise-to-span ratio of 1/13.5 and
a circular arch shape. Three of the spans align with those in the previous study, while
a shorter span is included to assess the viability of cassette elements for shorter bridges.
The preliminary calculations are conducted for a single 1.75 m wide PC arch. An overview
of the analyzed bridge spans and the resulting arch geometry is provided in Table 5.1.

adjusted for each case.

For this design, PC elements with a length of Lel = 4.0 m are designed, leaving approxi-
mately Lab = 1.7 m for each of the abutment elements. Although calculations presented
in Fig. 4.15 indicate that an element length of up to 6.0 m is possible, a reduced length
results in a more balanced internal force distribution between the MID and END sections
of each element (Fig. 4.5).
The geometry of the cassette element is configured based on the conclusions from Sec-
tion 4, where dimensions with the lowest impact on load bearing capacity are chosen as

cable size, utilizing increased rib height for higher material efficiency.

5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
5.2
Loads
As introduced in Section 2.4.3, a sandwich arch bridge utilizes the sandwich principle
to significantly increase its load bearing capacity.
However, it only becomes effective
after the entire section has been built. Therefore, to verify the design limit states of the
various structural components, different construction phases are considered. Generally,
the structural response of a PC arch sandwich bridge is viewed as a two component system,
where the entire dead load is carried by the PC arch, and all live loads are resisted through
a compression and tension force couple of the sandwich section.
The following parts of this section describe the load cases applicable to the design of the
PC arch sandwich bridge and present their arrangement across the span.
5.2.1
Dead loads
5.2.1.1
Self-weight of the PC arch
A simplified dead load distribution for a single PC element is calculated using Eq. 5.2,
where Lel is the cassette element length, and mc and ms represent the self-weights of
concrete and reinforcing steel, respectively. These are based on the cassette cross-section
in Fig. 5.2 and are calculated according to Eq. 5.3 and 5.4, where As.w represents the
amount of cross-reinforcement in the slab and transverse walls.

(5.3)

(5.4)
To account for the curvature of the arch and its effect on dead load distribution, a projec-
tion factor, as described by Eq. 5.5, is applied. This results in a characteristic dead load
distribution given by Eq. 5.6 and illustrated in Fig. 5.3.

5.2.1.2
Self-weight of the backfill
The self-weight of the backfill material varies across the span and depends on the shape
of the arch, rise-to-span ratio, and backfill height above the crown. Given the complex
geometry of the cassette PC arch, a conservative approximation is applied, assuming that
the cassette section follows the exact curvature of the PT cable, and the end transverse
walls of the cassette are neglected, assuming a uniform cross-section throughout.
Therefore, the height of the entire sandwich section can be described as given by Eq. 5.7,
where hbf.c is the backfill height above the crown, measured from the top of the cassette
element and Hc.top is the thickness of the concrete top plate. Then, Eq. 5.8 is used to
calculate the cross-sectional area of the pervious concrete along the span, with ASB.pc.c
and ASB.pc representing the cross-sectional areas within the bay of the cassette element
and the rest of the sandwich section, respectively.

of the arch has reached a higher backfill height than the other, generating both larger
positive and negative bending moments. To minimize this, pouring should be done in
incremental steps of a certain height increase. The critical step occurs when the first side
has reached the crown height, while the other is one step behind. Assuming an incremental
step of 0.25 m, the load distribution for such circumstances can be described by Eq. 5.12,

5.2.2

In this study, only traffic loads are considered for the live loads of the structure, according
to the load models in Section 2.5.2. Based on EC1, Part 2, 6.3.2 [28], the full width of
a notional lane for live traffic loads is 3.00 m, while in these calculations a single 1.75 m
wide PC arch is considered. Therefore, Eq. 2.11 for LM1 TS is modified with an axle
reduction factor kaxle = 0.5, accounting only for one side of the axle loads, assuming that
the other half would be carried by the adjacent arch. The resulting values for LM1 loads
are described by Eq. 5.17 and 5.18.
Q1k = ╬▒Q1 ┬╖ Q1k ┬╖ kaxle = 150.00 kN

q1k = ╬▒q1 ┬╖ q1k = 6.03 kN/m2

(5.21)
Both load models are arranged on the span to generate the most unfavorable conditions,
resulting in largest internal forces. For LM1, the two critical arrangements, as illustrated
in Fig. 5.8, are when the TS is positioned at the quarter point together with the UDL
system covering the corresponding half of the span, and when the TS is positioned at the
middle of the span with the UDL system covering the entire length of the bridge.
