5.2.2.1
LM3 placement analysis
The total length of the applicable special vehicle in LM3 is 20.4 m. To identify the vehicle
placement generating the largest internal forces, four different LM3 arrangements are
analyzed, considering the critical scenarios for both full beam (B) and arch (A) behavior
of the sandwich structure. This is particularly important for bridges of shorter spans,
where the special vehicle may cover the entire span or even exceed the length of the
bridge.
The four scenarios include the resulting force for the entire special vehicle (SV) placed
at the middle (L/2) and as close as possible to the quarter point of the span (L/4), and
the same for the resulting force of the carriage (C) axle loads specifically, given their
significantly larger loads per axle. The different load model arrangements for a bridge
span of 27.0 m are illustrated in Fig. 5.9.
Figure 5.9 | LM3 arrangement analysis (L=27.0m)
52
==PAGE:71==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
Fig. 5.10 presents the results of this analysis, showing that for both full beam and arch
behavior, the two arrangements with the SV carriage at the mid-span and quarter point
locations are sufficient to capture the critical load conditions within the structure. There-
fore, only these two arrangements are considered in further calculations.
Figure 5.10 | Bending moment distributions for the LM3 placement scenarios in Fig. 5.9
5.2.3
Post-Tensioning forces
In this case, a double end stressing approach is considered for both the concrete top
plate and the PC arch. This approach minimizes friction losses and ensures a more even
distribution of prestress forces. The maximum stressing force is determined according to
EC2, Part 1-1, 5.10.2.1 [31] and is described by Eq. 5.22, where Fpk and Fp0.1k are the
breaking and 0.1% proof loads per single strand.
Fp.max = min(0.8 ┬╖ Fpk, 0.9 ┬╖ Fp0.1k) = 220.59 kN

5.2.3.1
Friction losses
Friction losses constitute the majority of prestress losses in a PT system and are calculated
to determine the effective prestress force along the cable profile. This is done according to
the equation given in EC2, Part 1-1, 5.10.5.2 [31], as described by Eq. 5.25, where Pmax
corresponds to the maximum stressing force for a given cable size, and ╬╕(x) is the sum
of the angular displacement over a distance x. The angular displacement is calculated
based on the cable profile, in this case arch shape function yc(x), according to Eq. 5.26.
Here, ┬╡ = 0.19 is the coefficient of friction and k = 0.01 rad/m is the factor accounting for
unintended angular displacement. Both values are selected according to the CCL ETA
[33], assuming the use of non-lubricated strands and corrugated metal ducts.

5.2.3.2
Wedge seating losses
When the desired prestressing load is applied, the anchorage wedges are seated in the
wedge plate, clamping on the strands. This process results in an elongation loss, which,
according to the manufacturerтАЩs ETA [33], is defined as тИЖlws = 6 mm when a hydraulic
jack is equipped with a hydraulic lock-off mechanism.
Consequently, the loss of elongation results in a loss of prestress. The slope of the seating
loss function is equal to that of the friction loss function тИЖP┬╡(x), but of the opposite sign.
Therefore, the extent of this effect is determined according to Eq. 5.27, with the resulting
prestress loss at the anchorage calculated by Eq. 5.28.

deformation due to creep and shrinkage. This interaction can generally and approximately
be taken into account by a reduction factor of 0.8 [31]. Therefore, the effective prestress
along the length of the cable profile is described by Eq. 5.32 and 5.33 accounting for short
and long-term effects, respectively.

5.2.3.6
Abutment elements
The spreading of concentrated PT forces induces transverse tensile stresses within the
anchorage zone, requiring large amounts of transverse reinforcement, known as bursting
and anchorage zone reinforcement. To accommodate this, abutment elements in solid
concrete, as used for other PC arches, illustrated in Fig. 5.13 [42], must be designed
according to EC2, Part 1-1, 8.10.3 [31] and the PT system manufacturerтАЩs ETA [33, 34].
While detailed design for anchorage zone reinforcement is outside the scope of this study,
the general principle illustrated in Fig. 5.13 is also applicable to the cassette PC arch.
Therefore, the thickness of the abutment element within the anchorage zone is selected
according to the ETA guidelines [33] and the minimum concrete cover requirements in
Table 5.2. The remaining length is tapered to meet the dimensions of the following PC
element, effectively distributing the compressive loads across the entire section.
55
==PAGE:74==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
Figure 5.13 | An example of an abutment element used in a PC arch [42]

5.3
ULS Calculations
A sandwich arch bridge is a complex structural system that combines the benefits of both
sandwich and arch structures.
However, accurately predicting its structural behavior
under external loading is challenging. Typically, for a lower-bound solution, either beam
57
==PAGE:76==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
or arch behavior is assumed for the complete structure, while in practice, the true response
likely lies somewhere between the two extremes.
For the following calculations, it is assumed that the sandwich arch fully exhibits the
structural behavior of an arch structure. Additionally, the interfaces between the top and
bottom concrete elements and the backfill layer are considered to be capable of full shear
transfer, resulting in fully composite action of the sandwich section.
5.3.1
Sandwich section neutral axis
For an accurate assessment of the internal force distribution from external loads, the NA
of the entire sandwich section is calculated. This method is similar to the one described
in Section 4.2.1, where all the individual components illustrated in Fig. 5.2 are accounted
for. Due to the varying geometry of the cassette element and consequently the PC arch,
the theoretical position of the NA is calculated for three of the critical cassette sections,
as presented in Fig. 5.14.
Figure 5.14 | Neutral axis of a sandwich bridge section in Fig. 5.2 (L=27.0m)
As anticipated, the NA for the END and MID sections are practically overlapping, while
the WALL section results in a lower placement. In practice, the effective NA would vary
along the span between these identified values. Therefore, as a simplification, the average
of the three is assumed for further calculations. Furthermore, nearing the abutments, the
calculated NA would deviate from this identified line towards the support hinge location.
However, for this analysis, it is assumed to continue with the calculated average, meaning
that any horizontal support reactions are applied at the NA level.
5.3.2
Internal forces in the Pearl-Chain arch
For load cases describing the dead loads of the structure, the general approach to arch
internal force calculations, as covered in Section 2.1.3, is applied. An exception to this
applies for load combination LC1, as covered further below.
5.3.2.1
Arch lifting
The individual PC elements are delivered to the site, where assembly through the appli-
cation of PT forces takes place, followed by lifting the arch into its designated location.
58
==PAGE:77==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
Throughout this process, the arch must be capable of resisting any internal forces that
may arise.
The internal forces are calculated using the dead load distribution depicted in Fig. 5.3.
In this case, a three-point lifting rig is considered, with lifting point locations assumed
as shown in Fig. 5.15. While the exact number and positioning may be adjusted based
on the arch size, they are selected to ensure similar vertical reaction forces, resulting in
lower and more evenly distributed bending moments.
Figure 5.15 | Static system during arch lifting (L=27.0m)
According to the influence length of each lifting point and assuming no horizontal re-
actions, the vertical reaction forces are calculated using simple equilibrium equations.
For this case, the reaction forces are found as follows: VLC1.1 = VLC1.3 = 75.85 kN and
VLC1.2 = 74.40 kN. With no horizontal reactions, the resulting bending moment is cal-
culated equivalent to that of a beam. Shear and normal force distributions are found
through force decomposition, as previously described by Eq. 2.8 and 2.9.
5.3.2.2
Live load response
For live loads, the additional bending moment is resisted through the force couple in the
completed sandwich section, while shear forces are assumed to be entirely resisted by the
backfill layer, as illustrated in Fig. 5.16 (a).
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.16 | (a) Live load response in a sandwich section and (b) force decomposition for a
PC arch
The bending moment from live loads is calculated according to the assumed arch behavior
of the sandwich structure and the corresponding NA of the entire section, as identified in
Fig. 5.14.
59
==PAGE:78==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
With bending moment distribution obtained, Eq. 5.34 is applied to calculate the live
load contribution to the total normal force in the PC arch. This is done by dividing the
bending moment value by the distance between the two forces in the couple, which in
this case are assumed to be positioned at the location of the top plate and PC element
centroids, as shown in Fig. 5.16 (a). Additionally, accounting for the arch curvature, force
decomposition, as illustrated in Fig. 5.16 (b), is applied. Consequently, a fraction of the
load carried by the arch is transformed into shear, as described by Eq. 5.35.
NEd.PC.LL(x) = MEd(x)
li(x)
┬╖ cos(╧Ж(x))
(5.34)
QEd.PC.LL(x) = MEd(x)
li(x)
┬╖ sin(╧Ж(x))
(5.35)
5.3.2.3
Combined structural response
The combined structural response for the PC arch is obtained through Eq. 5.36 - 5.38,
where the effects of dead load (DL), live load (LL), and prestressing (PT) are superimposed
according to the specific load combination. The resulting internal forces in the PC arch
for the ULS load combinations LC1 to LC9 are presented in Fig. 5.17 - 5.19.
MEd.PC(x) = MEd.PC.DL(x)
(5.36)
QEd.PC(x) = QEd.PC.DL(x) + QEd.PC.LL(x)
(5.37)
NEd.PC(x) = NEd.PC.DL(x) + NEd.PC.LL(x) + NEd.PT.PC(x)
(5.38)
The results show that LC3, corresponding to the critical condition of backfill pouring,
generates the largest bending moments by a significant margin, with the total dead load
of a complete sandwich section being the second largest. Similarly, LC3 results in the
largest shear forces near the abutments, while live traffic loads cause the largest peaks
in the mid and quarter points of the span for both shear and normal forces. Specifically,
traffic loads from LM1 result in larger internal forces throughout the span compared to
those from LM3.

5.3.3
Internal forces in the top plate
Applying the same principle as for the PC arch, the live load contribution to the total
normal force in the concrete top plate is calculated according to Eq. 5.39.
Since the
top plate is constructed on a horizontal plane, the entire load is resisted through normal
stresses in the concrete section. Consequently, Eq. 5.40 describes the total normal force,
combining the effects of live loads (LL) and prestressing (PT). The results for LC6 to
LC9 (traffic loads) are presented in Fig. 5.20.
Figure 5.20 | Total normal force distribution in top plate (L=27.0m)

Similar to the results for the PC arch, the traffic loads from LM1 generate both the largest
compressive and tensile forces in the top plate. However, due to the applied prestressing,
the cross-section remains under compression throughout.
5.3.4
Internal forces in the backfill layer
With the entire shear force generated by the live loads resisted by the backfill layer, the
resulting shear distribution is calculated according to Eq. 5.41, where VA represents the
arch vertical support reaction, and qEd and QEd are the design distributed and point loads,
respectively. The results for load combinations LC6 to LC9 are presented in Fig. 5.21.

5.3.5
Design capacities
To verify the design of the PC arch sandwich bridge, the calculated internal forces are
compared against the design resistances of each component.
5.3.5.1
PC arch normal force and bending moment resistance
Following the method described in Section 4.2, the bending moment resistances at various
levels of applied normal forces are calculated for each of the critical cassette sections,
according to the specific cassette element in Fig. 5.2. For this calculation, the applied
prestressing forces account for long-term losses identified in Section 5.2.3. The resulting
design limits are illustrated by Fig. 5.22 for both positive and negative bending moments.
62
==PAGE:81==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.22 | Normal force - bending moment interaction diagrams of the critical cassette
sections for (a) positive and (b) negative bending moments
As expected, the critical sections WALL and JOINT ensure significantly larger load bear-
ing capacities compared to the END and MID sections. The latter two also show slight
differences between them, primarily due to the variation in PT cable placement within
the section. Consequently, the curves for END and MID sections are used to determine
the design load limits. Additionally, for negative bending moment, the JOINT section is
also considered, as an overlap at lower normal forces is observed in Fig. 5.22(b).
The internal force distributions from Fig. 5.17 and 5.19 for bending moment and normal
force, respectively, are then plotted against the identified design limits. The results are
presented in Fig. 5.23 for positive bending moment and Fig. 5.24 for negative bending
moment. As seen from the results, the load combination corresponding to the critical
condition during backfill pouring (LC3) generates the largest loads on the PC arch struc-
ture, closely approaching the design limits. However, all of the load combinations remain
within the identified limits.
Figure 5.23 | Design load verification against design M-N diagram for positive bending mo-
ment (L=27.0m)

==PAGE:82==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
Figure 5.24 | Design load verification against design M-N diagram for negative bending mo-
ment (L=27.0m)
5.3.5.2
PC arch shear resistance
The shear resistance of all critical sections is calculated for a member without design
shear reinforcement. Naturally, sections WALL and JOINT ensure much larger resistances
compared to END and MID, due to increased concrete area. For the cassette element in
Fig. 5.2, the calculated resistances are QRd.c.END = 177.04 kN and QRd.c.MID = 159.39 kN,
for the critical sections END and MID, respectively. Fig. 5.25 compares the lowest of
the two, as a limiting value, against the design shear forces arising from all ULS load
combinations, confirming that the cassette element is able to resist the applied loads
without requiring design shear reinforcement.
Figure 5.25 | Design shear force verification against shear resistance of the cassette element
(L=27.0m)
Note: The design shear force from LC3 at the left support exceeds the highlighted shear
resistance (QRd.c.MID). However, the first PC element at each abutment (shaded area) is
a highly reinforced solid concrete element, as previously covered in Section 5.2.3, ensuring
the required shear capacity. For comparison, the shear resistance of the cassette WALL
section without design shear reinforcement is QRd.c.WALL = 503.47 kN.
5.3.5.3
Top plate normal force resistance
The compressive force resistance for the concrete top plate is determined using the same
approach as for the cassette section, covered in Section 4.2. The general approach for the
64
==PAGE:83==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
top plate involves supplying sufficient amount of prestressing to keep the concrete section
under compression at all times.
This approach prevents concrete cracking and water
ingress, therefore preventing accelerated deterioration due to adverse weather conditions.
For the concrete top plate in Fig. 5.2, with a height of Hc.top = 120 mm and width equal
to that of the PC arch, the compressive normal force resistance for a concrete strength
class C30/37 is found to be NRd.top = 3615 kN. As depicted in Fig. 5.26, this value ensures
that the design normal forces remain within the limits for all relevant load combinations.
Figure 5.26 | Design normal force verification against normal force resistance of the concrete
top plate (L=27.0m)
5.3.5.4
Backfill layer shear resistance
The shear resistance of the backfill layer is calculated according to Eq. 5.42, considering
the varying cross-section of the layer as determined by Eq. 5.8 and the material properties
of the pervious concrete. Here, ASB.pc(x) describes the cross-sectional area corresponding
to a vertical section of the bridge. This approach is slightly conservative, as sections
analyzed perpendicular to the NA of the sandwich section in Fig. 5.14 result in a larger
cross-sectional area and thus greater shear resistance.
QRd.bf(x) = fpcvd ┬╖ ASB.pc(x)
(5.42)
Figure 5.27 | Design shear force verification against shear resistance of the backfill layer
(L=27.0m)
65
==PAGE:84==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
Fig. 5.27 presents the calculated shear resistance of the backfill layer and the design shear
forces for load combinations LC6 to LC9. These results show that even for a shallow
arch, the shear resistance at the abutments is multiple times larger than the design forces.
Therefore, for the considered live loads and their respective arrangements, the calculated
shear forces near the crown section are used as dimensioning values.
5.3.6
Cassette cross-reinforcement
To ensure the functionality of the cassette element and its ability to resist the external
loads, additional cross-reinforcement is provided for the slab and transverse wall parts. For
the slab, the required amount of reinforcement is determined by considering the backfill
pouring procedure. During this phase, the slab must resist the dead load of the backfill
material, with the highest load applied to the first cassette PC element, right after the
abutment elements, where the cross-section of the backfill layer is the largest.
For this calculation, a free body diagram (FBD), as illustrated in Fig. 5.28, is considered.
Here, qbf.crit represents the largest expected load over a certain length of the PC arch.
The required amount of reinforcement is calculated based on the force couple approach
in a reinforced concrete section.
Figure 5.28 | Free body diagram for slab cross-reinforcement calculation
For the transverse walls, reinforcement is provided to ensure they are able to resist the
loads arising from shear force transformation, generated by the placement of shear keys
along the length of the wall, as illustrated in Fig. 5.29.
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.29 | (a) Shear key load distribution principle and (b) free body diagram for trans-
verse wall cross-reinforcement calculations
66
==PAGE:85==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
Using the force triangle, the force acting perpendicular to the transverse wall is calculated.
The FBD in Fig. 5.29 (b) is applied, assuming PT cables resist the support reactions in
tension, while the horizontal component of the transformed shear force generates bending
moment in the transverse wall, assuming uniform load distribution along the entire length
of the shear key. Similarly, the required reinforcement is calculated by the force couple
approach, which in this case is conservative because, in practice, the transverse wall is
supported along its bottom side.
The UDL for this scenario is approximated as described by Eq. 5.43, where Qmax repre-
sents the largest design shear force at the location of a shear key, as found in Fig. 5.18,
and ╬▒ is the shear key pocket angle, as illustrated in Fig. 5.29 (a).
qQ.crit = tan(╬▒) ┬╖ Qmax
Wwall
(5.43)
It is found that for the PC arch sandwich bridge depicted in Fig. 5.1 and 5.2, four 10 mm
bars per meter or Asw.slab = 3.16 cm2/m are required for the slab to resist the applied dead
load of the backfill. Additionally, three 10 mm bars or Asw.wall = 2.37 cm2 are supplied
for each of the transverse walls, which have a thickness of 110 mm. Detailed calculations
for both cases are provided in Appendix F.

reinforcement.

Therefore, for the cassette elements, which are subjected to both, stress values are calcu-
lated for top and bottom fibers of the section. Conversely, for the top plate, the equation
is simplified to account only for the normal force components, including post-tensioning.
Here, ╧Гc.pre and Mp.pre represent the compressive stress in the concrete and the bending
moment induced by the pre-tensioning strands, calculated according to Eq. 5.46 and
5.47, respectively. Meanwhile, Mp.pt(x) describes the bending moment induced by the
post-tensioning cables, calculated according to Eq. 5.48, where PEd(x) represents the PT
design force distribution along the cable profile. This parameter accounts for either short
or long-term losses, depending on the specific load combination.
╧Гc.pre = Fp.pre.top + Fp.pre.bot
Ai
(5.46)
Mp.pre = Fp.pre.top ┬╖ (zp.pre.top тИТzib) + Fp.pre.bot ┬╖ (zp.pre.bot тИТzib)
(5.47)
Mp.pt(x) = PEd(x) ┬╖ (zp.pt тИТzib)
(5.48)
The relevant sectional parameters are calculated according to Eq. 4.10 and Eq. 4.11 for
the section modulus of the top and bottom fibers, respectively, and Eq. 4.3 for the ideal
concrete cross-sectional area. For the cassette element, the critical sections END and
MID are considered for dimensioning purposes due to their smaller cross-sectional areas
and section moduli.
The resulting stress distributions in the PC arch and the top plate, according to the SLS
load combinations in Table 5.4, are presented in Fig. 5.31 and 5.32, for the END and MID
sections of the cassette element, respectively, and in Fig. 5.33 for the concrete top plate.
All figures also include the SLS stress limits as outlined in Table 5.6.

As anticipated based on the results from ULS calculations, load combination LC12, rep-
resenting the critical condition during backfill pouring, yields the largest stresses for both
the top and bottom fibers of the cassette sections. However, stress values for all load
combinations remain within the limits of SLS. Among the two analyzed sections, the
MID section experiences higher stresses due to the PT cable placement with a larger ec-
centricity, resulting in greater positive bending moments compared to the END section.
Additionally, the top plate remains under compression for all considered load combina-
tions, with the compressive stresses also well within the limits.

The design verification for the 15.0 m span revealed that the cassette approach resulted
in an over-dimensioned PC arch. This is primarily due to requirements for minimum
concrete cover, PT cable curvature, and the dimensions of the prestressing systems. To
address this, the CCL XF-10 flat cable was considered for PT [34], allowing for reduced
rib height and width. Nevertheless, both ULS and SLS calculations indicate that the
resulting section exhibits a significant bearing capacity reserve.
Conversely, for larger spans (тЙе35.0 m), maintaining the minimum cassette rib width ap-
proach resulted in significantly taller cassette sections. This increase in height resulted in
greater quantities for backfill material, therefore increasing the dead load and the struc-
tural requirements of the cassette element. As a result, it was found that simultaneously
increasing the width of the ribs had a favorable overall effect on the design. Despite pro-
viding lower bending moment resistance for the same amount of material, this approach
results in much lower backfill consumption, reducing the costs and environmental impact.

==PAGE:90==
5 Sandwich arch bridge analysis
Fig. 5.34 illustrates the comparison between the cassette and SL-Deck designs [13], pre-
senting the total GWP per single arch of equal width. The data points are extrapolated
using a power law function fit, highlighting that the cassette design is more material effi-
cient across the range of span lengths considered. The cassette design results in 14% less
CO2 emissions for a 27.0 m span bridge and up to 16% less for a 50.0 m span. Additionally,
the study on SL-Deck PC arch sandwich bridges concluded that it only becomes more
material efficient for span lengths above 25.0 m, with an estimated 40% difference in total
GWP for a span length of 15.0 m [13]. The approximation in Fig. 5.34 shows a similar
difference between the cassette and SL-Deck designs, suggesting that the cassette design
is more competitive even for shorter span arches. A detailed overview of this comparison
is provided in Appendix G.
Fig. 5.35 provides further insight into the individual GWP contributions of the different
bridge components. It shows a consistent trend across the comparable span lengths with
the cassette design producing lower GWP for the PC arch compared to the SL-Deck, with
the difference increasing with span length. Lower GWP is also achieved for the concrete
top plate, however, likely due to the design choice of using flat cables, which allow for
reduced section height. Conversely, the cassette design uses more backfill material than
the SL-Deck sections because the bay volume of the cassette must be filled for the sandwich
effect. However, the lower center of gravity for the cassette elements increases the lever
arm for the sandwich force couple, leading to reduced normal forces without requiring
additional backfill above the cassette section, while still providing enough material for the
required shear resistance.
Figure 5.35 | Distribution of GWP (A1 - A3) among the components of the bridge
5.5.2
Beam behavior in sandwich arch bridges
The design parameters in Table 5.7 and the corresponding GWP in Fig. 5.34 and 5.35 are
characteristic for cassette PC arch bridges assuming full arch behavior. As highlighted
by Fig. 5.9, this assumption significantly affects the bending moment distribution for live
loads due to the lack of horizontal support reactions. To investigate the impact of assumed
structural behavior on material efficiency, another design calculation was conducted for
the 27.0 m span, assuming full beam behavior. Table 5.9 highlights the differences in
design parameters between the two assumptions (beam and arch behavior), while the
remaining parameters are as indicated originally in Table 5.7.

The results in Table 5.9 show that for the design assuming beam behavior, a significant
increase in backfill height is required for the sandwich section to resist the larger bend-
ing moments generated by the live loads. Furthermore, additional backfill increases the
dead load of the structure, requiring greater bending moment resistance of the PC arch.
Consequently, the rib height and width, along with the number of PT strands, have been
increased.
According to theory, the top plate should not experience any tensile forces, therefore no
PT is supplied, and the plate is dimensioned based on the calculated compressive forces.
Figure 5.36 | GWP (A1 - A3) for the assumed arch and beam behaviors (L=27.0m)
In Fig. 5.36 the resulting GWP values for the two design assumptions are compared.
As anticipated based on the results in Table 5.9, there is a significant increase of ap-
proximately 26% in GWP for the design assuming beam behavior. This highlights the
disadvantage of choosing beam behavior as the lower bound solution for a sandwich arch
bridge.

==PAGE:92==
6 Conclusions
6
Conclusions
The primary objective of this thesis was to develop an optimized Pearl-Chain element to
improve material efficiency and sustainability in bridge construction. The hypothesis was
that precast concrete cassette elements would offer a more sustainable design for PC arch
sandwich bridges compared to the SL-Deck elements.
Through analytical calculations, it was determined that a two-hinged static system for
the PC arch provides optimal internal force distribution for a closed-spandrel arch bridge.
However, due to the specifics of the closed-spandrel systems, this proves difficult to con-
struct. Consequently, the design is based on a two-hinged system, while in practice, it
exhibits structural behavior somewhere between a two-hinged and a fixed arch, which
is a conservative approach. An optimal rise-to-span ratio between 1/10 and 1/14 was
identified, resulting in a reduced and more uniform internal force distribution. Within
this range, circular arch shapes are found preferable, enabling the prefabrication of equal
PC concrete elements. Increasing the backfill height above the crown slightly reduces the
bending moment but significantly raises the normal compressive forces.
The investigation of the cassette elements revealed that increasing prestressing reinforce-
ment is the most material efficient and sustainable approach to increase the bending
moment resistance, followed by an increase in concrete rib height, while minimizing the
rest of the dimensions. A cassette element equivalent in both positive and negative mo-
ment resistances was found to achieve 26% less CO2 emissions compared to the SL-Deck,
showcasing its potential for improved sustainability.
Comparing complete PC arch sandwich bridges of equal spans, the cassette design was
found to be considerably more material efficient, with GWP reductions of 14% to 16% for
27 and 50 meter spans, respectively. The cassette design resulted in lower CO2 emissions
for the PC arch itself but a higher amount for the backfill. This is due to the bay section
of the cassette, requiring it to be filled for the sandwich action to take place. However,
as a result, the lower center of gravity in the cassette elements increases the lever arm
between the compressive and tensile forces in the force couple. This leads to reduced
normal forces in the arch and the top plate without the need for additional backfill on
top of the cassette section, resulting in a more sustainable solution. For larger spans,
increasing rib width along with the height proved to be a more efficient approach due to
a reduction in the dead load of the backfill.
Finally, considering full beam behavior for the lower bound solution of the sandwich
arch was found to be undesirable, resulting in a 26% GWP increase for a 27 meter span
compared to an equivalent design assuming arch behavior.
74
==PAGE:93==
6 Conclusions
Further studies
The development of this thesis has inspired further research topics that could advance the
sustainability aspect of PC arch sandwich bridges even further:
тАв An empirical study to determine the exact benefit of the cassette geometry for
improved shear transfer between the sandwich layers, potentially allowing the use
of materials with lesser properties for the backfill layer.
тАв Further optimization of the cassette cross-section, focusing on the position of the slab
along the vertical axis, resulting in an H-shaped cross-section. This optimization
can lead to a more balanced section in terms of positive and negative moment
resistances and minimize backfill material consumption, while still offering improved
shear transfer.
тАв Development of a hinge system that spans the entire depth of the sandwich section,
maintaining a two-hinged static system even after the entire section has been con-
structed. Such innovation would improve the structural predictability and allow for
more accurate design calculations.
75

A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels

==PAGE:99==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
Purpose: This report is a literature review conducted as an introduction for the MasterтАЩs
thesis project "Development of concrete sandwich bridge system". The primary objective
is to establish a fundamental understanding of core principles and different parameter
influence on sandwich panel structural behavior. Refer to Appendix A for a categorized
literature overview.
1
Introduction
Sandwich panels are multilayered structures consisting of one or more high-strength and
stiffness layers called faces (or skins, wythes), bonded to one or more flexible layers with
considerably lower density, called the core. As illustrated in Fig. 1, the faces are typically
made from identical materials and thickness. This configuration, similar to an I-beam,
results in increased strength and stiffness without a substantial increase in weight. In this
analogy, the faces act as flanges, primarily carrying load in tension/compression, while
the core, similar to a sectionтАЩs web, provides structural support, increases bending and
torsional stiffness and carries the majority of the shear load [1тАУ3].
Figure 1 | Illustration sandwich plate geometry [3].
The concept of sandwich structures is not a recent development. Its origins can be
traced back to the middle of the last century (1849), although the principles of sandwich
structures may have been applied even earlier. The widespread adoption of the sandwich
concept gained traction during World War II, primarily for use in aircraft structures.
Since then, it has evolved, extending to various applications such as satellites, ships, cars,
railway vehicles, wind energy systems, bridge and building construction and more [2, 4].
Researchers are continuously exploring new construction materials and methods for
improved performance, durability and efficiency. In this context, sandwich structures have
demonstrated their potential, offering material efficiency in lightweight design, high load
bearing capacity, low thermal conductivity and rapid installation [3, 5].
Nowadays, sandwich structures, along with other lightweight construction concepts,
are getting increased attention. This is driven by global efforts to minimize the carbon
footprint associated with building construction and operation, which accounts for ap-
proximately 39% of energy related global CO2 emissions. Of this, 28% are attributed to
1
A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels
81
==PAGE:100==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
operational energy consumption and 11% to the energy used in producing building and
construction materials [6]. The various advantages offered by sandwich structures, com-
bined with advancements in new materials and the growing demand for high-performance,
lightweight structures ensure that sandwich construction will continue to be in high de-
mand [4].
2
Sandwich panel types
2.1
According to layer composition
While the working principle remains the same conceptually, sandwich structures can be
categorized based on key characteristics, namely, layer composition and core assembly.
According to layer composition, three sandwich construction categories can be identified:
an ordinary sandwich, consisting of two outer faces surrounding a single lightweight core
(as identified in Fig. 1), an open-face sandwich or semi-sandwich, consisting of only one
face and a core; and a multi-layer sandwich, which consists of multiple cores and fac-
ings [4]. Concepts for both open-face and multi-layer sandwich panels are illustrated in
Fig. 2. Among these, ordinary sandwich panels, including a variation with a multilayered
composite core [7], are the most commonly used.
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 2 | (a) Ordinary [8], (b) Open-face and (c) multi-layer sandwich concepts [2].
Furthermore, while ordinary sandwich panels typically have two faces of the same
material, fiber orientation and thickness, in special cases, such as when used for a building
envelope, the faces can differ in one of these parameters or in any combination of all three.
When both faces are matching, the panel is regarded as a mid-plane symmetric sandwich,
otherwise, it is considered a mid-plane asymmetric sandwich [4].
2.2
According to core assembly
The core of a sandwich structure can be composed of almost any material and architecture.
Generally, two groups are distinguished: homogeneous and structured core materials.
Homogeneous materials are further divided into solid and foam cores, while structured
core materials are categorized into honeycomb, corrugated, and textile cores [8]. Section 3
offers a more detailed exploration of specific material uses and combinations.
2
A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels
82
==PAGE:101==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
Figure 3 | Core assembly groups and types [8].
3
Materials and layer composition
3.1
Faces and core
A variety of design configurations have been explored for sandwich panels. As outlined
in Section 2, materials with high compressive/tensile strength and stiffness such as con-
crete, wood, high-density polymers, aluminum, steel and various other metal alloys are
commonly used for sandwich faces [1, 2, 4]. Recent studies have also investigated the
feasibility of such composite materials as glass and carbon fiber reinforced concrete [5, 7,
9].
The variety in material selection for different core configurations highlights an even
wider range of possibilities. These usually include low-density materials, such as solid
foams and flexible thermosetting resins containing lightweight fillers. For example, ho-
mogeneous core materials, namely polyurethane (PU) and expanded polystyrene (EPS)
foams are widely used in automotive and construction industries, respectively [2, 8]. Con-
versely, a solid core approach is used in a specific type of glued laminated timber - com-
bined glulam, which utilizes lower strength laminates for the inner zone of the structural
member [10].
Alternatively, expanded high-density materials in cellular form (honeycomb or web
core) or corrugated form (truss, hyperbolic paraboloid, and corrugated sheet) are also
widely utilized. High-density materials used for this purpose include aluminum, titanium,
steel and fibrous composites. Structured cores are commonly bonded to the face plates
using adhesives, while cases with a metal-metal interface, as seen in textile core panels
(Fig. 3) involve the use of welding or brazing methods [2, 11]. Honeycomb core materials
have been widely used in aerospace industries for many decades as the preferred material
for buckling and bending sensitive sandwich structures. In contrast to corrugated core
types, which have cell openings in the in-plane direction, honeycomb core types only have
3
A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels
83
==PAGE:102==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
openings in the out-of-plane direction. A practical and well-known example of corrugated
sheet cores is a cardboard sheet [4, 8].
3.2
Optimized material use
As mentioned earlier, sandwich structures are material efficient, allowing for substantial
weight and cost reductions by utilizing high strength materials only where structural
integrity demands it. Fig. 4 showcases the potential for material efficiency and savings in
weight [12].
Figure 4 | Example for material efficiency of a sandwich material combination [12].
From this example, it might seem intuitive to increase the thickness ratio of core
to face layers (hc/hf) as much as practically feasible for a given application, aiming for
maximum weight and cost savings. However, it is important to note that this particular
example assumes full composite action and it is essential that the core material possesses
sufficient mechanical properties to ensure it. The coreтАЩs primary role is to prevent the
movement of the faces relative to each other, both in-plane (with sufficient out-of-plane
shear properties) and out-of-plane (with sufficient out-of-plane compression/tension prop-
erties) [12, 13].
The further apart the faces are, the harder it becomes for the panel to achieve full
composite action, making partial composite action more likely. Therefore, a significant
increase in the coreтАЩs thickness diminishes structural efficiency and potentially alters the
failure mechanism. Fig. 5 presents the results from a study analyzing the influence of
insulation thickness on the maximum shear flow - an important parameter describing
the degree of composite action [3, 12тАУ17]. Further explanation on composite action is
provided in Section 4.
4
A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels
84
==PAGE:103==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
Conversely, face thickness is generally limited by a minimum value, determined by
the materialтАЩs compressive/tensile strength. For materials like steel reinforced concrete,
fire regulations and concrete cover requirements also affect this parameter [5, 13, 18тАУ20].
Given the diverse design configurations and variables influencing sandwich structure
performance, no straightforward rules of thumb exist for determining an efficient thick-
ness ratio between the faces and the core. While various studies propose mathematical
optimization techniques, achieving an optimal design ultimately depends on specific objec-
tives, design constraints and variables. Therefore, an iterative approach is often necessary
[8, 21]. Table 1 provides an overview of the core thickness to face ratio observed across var-
ious composite types, as obtained from the literature study summarized in Appendix A.
Furthermore, the table includes material strength parameters - compressive strength for
concrete and yield strength for metal face composites, to highlight potential correlation
with the thickness ratio.
Figure 5 | Maximum shear flow versus insulation thickness [14].
Table 1 reveals that, while the sample size is rather small, the standard deviations are
notably high, exceeding 50% of the average values. Additionally, the correlation between
the layer thickness ratio and face material strength is investigated. While certain cases
may seem to have similar configurations, these are mostly different specimens within the
same study or from the same author. The results show relatively low correlation scores,
except for the fiber reinforced concrete - foam concrete and metal - concrete compos-
ites, which, however, have a negligible sample size, making their reliability questionable.
Overall, this highlights the variability and lack of apparent trends indicating an optimal
layer thickness ratio for different sandwich compositions. The observed variability can
be attributed to the diverse potential layer compositions and the influence of the shear
connector performance - a key factor determining the failure mode in sandwich structures,
which imposes certain material requirements. Both of these aspects are further investi-
gated in Sections 4 and 5. These findings further reinforce the statement that an optimal
solution for a sandwich element relies on the particular use case and conditions.

4
Composite action
The degree of composite action in structural elements can be classified into fully compos-
ite, partially composite and non-composite. This classification is based on the shear flow
capacity determined by the bond and shear connector strength between the core and face
layers (refer to Fig. 6). A section is considered fully composite when all of the longitudinal
shear, as illustrated by the linear strain profile in Fig. 6 (a), is transferred between the
faces, resulting in all parts of the panel working together as a unit in resisting bending
moments. On the other hand, if there is no shear transfer, the section is non-composite,
as illustrated in Fig. 6 (c). Partial composite action falls somewhere between the two
extremes, involving only a fraction of shear transfer, thus allowing the faces to share load
but bend independently around their neutral axes (Fig. 6(b)). The schematic in Fig. 7
further illustrates the influence of different degrees of composite action on layer interaction
and the resulting deflections [13, 14, 22, 24, 29, 32].
Figure 6 | Strain distribution at different degrees of composite action [29]
.
Figure 7 | The influence of degree of composite action on load transfer and deflection.
To achieve optimal structural design for a sandwich panel, it is important that the
predicted behavior closely aligns with the actual structural response.
It is, however,
highly dependent on the degree of composite action attained by the panel.
While a
fully composite action would be optimal for both behavior predictability and material
7
A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels
87
==PAGE:106==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
use, practical challenges, particularly in achieving the required stiffness and strength for
shear connectors, often result in panels exhibiting only partial composite action. In such
instances, it becomes challenging to accurately predict the shear transfer between the
two faces through the core and connectors [13, 15, 24]. For this reason, ongoing studies
aim to develop precise computational models for accurate behavior prediction of partial
composite action and facilitate optimal design [2].
4.1
Shear connectors
Shear connectors, as previously established, are crucial for achieving high degree of com-
posite action in sandwich panels. Historically, steel shear connectors, such as bent bars
and steel wire trusses have been commonly used since 1990тАЩs [26]. However, despite the
potential of fully composite section, they faced drawbacks in high thermal conductivity
and corrosion risk leading to a shift towards Fiber-Reinforced Polymer (FRP) connectors.
FRP connectors have been in use for over thirty years and offer significantly lower thermal
conductivity and corrosion resistance, increasing service life [13, 14, 20].
FRP composites offer higher tensile strength but lower shear strength and buckling
resistance.
Therefore, to achieve composite action, FRP connectors in a combination
with insulation foam are arranged in a way that forms truss mechanism thus avoiding
shear stresses. In such arrangement, FRP connectors serve as tensile members, while the
rigid insulation provides the compressive members [13, 26]. While sandwich structures
using steel shear connectors generally exhibit superior performance, achieving nearly of
fully composite action, their substitution with FRP is a trade-off for improved thermal
insulation properties [24]. However, several FRP shear connectors have also demonstrated
promising results in achieving full or high degrees of composite action, depending on design
configurations. In certain cases, a reduced composite action can actually be advantageous,
limiting thermal bowing otherwise present in fully composite sections [20, 24, 29].
Figure 8 | Carbon FRP grid used as shear transfer mechanism [15]
.
8
A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels
88
==PAGE:107==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
Figure 9 | Common shear connector types (a) FRP mesh, (b) FRP or metallic truss, (c)
metallic pin, (d) metallic or FRP bent bar, (e) plastic pin, and (f) FRP pin [24]
.
Partially composite panels utilize various types of shear connectors - plate, box, truss,
grid (mesh), bent bar or pin connectors - made out of steel and various types of FRP,
such as glass FRP (GFRP), basalt FRP (BFRP) or carbon FRP (CFRP) [20]. These
fiber options differ in tensile strength, temperature and corrosion resistance, and cost.
CFRP, with the highest tensile capacity, is also the most expensive one, followed by
basalt and glass fibers [33, 34]. Common shear connector types are illustrated in Fig. 9.
Pin connectors generally provide lower degrees of composite action, while continuous truss
or FRP grid offers higher degrees [24].
The shear connectorтАЩs capacity, affecting the systemтАЩs strength and stiffness, relies
on material, geometry and design. Common failure modes are schematically illustrated in
Fig. 10. Using materials with higher tensile capacity or increasing the shear connection
reinforcement ratio improves the panelтАЩs performance [14, 24, 29].
Figure 10 | Connector failure modes (a) undeformed section, (b) connector yielding (metallic
only), (c) connector rupture, (d) connector pullout, (e) connector buckling, and (f) foam splitting
[24]
.

4.2
Calculation of degree of composite action
While there is currently no standardized method for empirically determining the degree
of composite action (DCA) in sandwich structures testing, various methods have been
proposed, displacement, load, and strain methods [26].
4.2.1
Displacement method

of composite action, comparing the experimentally determined moment of inertia of the
specimen (Iexp) with its theoretical values for fully composite (Ic) and non-composite
(Inc) sections. For three-point, four-point and uniform load bending, the experimental
moment of inertia can be calculated using equations 1, 2 and 3, respectively. Here P and

at the mid-span and E is the modulus of elasticity of the face material. The degree of
composite action in terms of initial stiffness is then calculated using Eq. (4) [35].

Load method
Conversely, the load method offers an alternative for calculating DCA at or beyond the
serviceability limit state (SLS), including at the yield point or, more importantly, at the
ultimate limit state (ULS). The DCA with the load method is calculated according to
Eq. (8), where Pnc, Pc and Pexp represent the selected loads at 0%, 100% and partial
composite action, respectively.
The load method typically results in lower degrees of
composite action compared to the stiffness methods due to the occurrence of different
failure mechanisms that degrade the composite action. As a result, however, it can be
beneficial in assessing specific design configurations, particularly shear connectors and
their shear transfer capacity at critical loads beyond the elastic deformation region [18,
26].

while a non-composite section assumes full/complete slip. Consequently, a partial degree
of composite action occurs when there is partial slip between the layers. The DCA us-
ing the strain method for sandwich panels is calculated according to Eq. (9), where ╬╡1

5
Failure modes
Figure 13 | Failure modes. (a) Face yielding/fracture, (b) core shear failure, (c and d) face
wrinkling, (e) general buckling, (f) shear crimping, (g) face dimpling and (h) local indentation
[21]
.
Given the diverse material combinations, sandwich structures may experience various fail-
ure modes, each defining a specific failure criteria, determining the load bearing capacity.
Depending on the degree of composite action [9, 26], geometry, material properties, and
loading and boundary conditions, different failure modes become critical. Understand-
ing these modes and their corresponding failure criteria is essential for efficient sandwich
13
A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels
93
==PAGE:112==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
design [21, 38]. Schematic illustrations of the most common sandwich failure modes are
presented in Fig. 13.
5.1
Face yielding or crushing
Every material has a maximum allowable compressive and tensile stress, leading to com-
pression fracture or yielding when these limits are reached. This failure mode is common
in sandwich structures with high or full composite action, occurring under pure bending
or bending with low shear, minimizing the likelihood of core shear failure [22, 29]. Com-
posite face materials are more prone to compressive failure given their lower strength in
compression compared to tension. Generally, sandwich core materials have much lower
stiffness than the faces, making the coreтАЩs contribution in this failure mode negligible [21,
39].
5.2
Core shear failure
As established in Section 1, sandwich core carries nearly the entire transverse force, there-
fore is primarily subjected to shear stress. Core shear failure occurs when the shear stress
exceeds the shear strength of the specific material. At this critical state, a field of tensile
stresses forms at a 45тЧжangle causing crack formation at a similar inclination. Such crack-
ing is typical for pure shear failure and is referred to as shear cracking. While shear stress
is typically the dominant one in the core for short-span members, in long-span members
the normal stresses can also reach a comparable level, leading to a bi-axial stress state.
Such scenario requires failure characterization of the core under combined stresses. Core
failure degrades its supporting role and serves as a precursor to other more catastrophic
failure modes, such as face wrinkling, discussed in Section 5.3 [21, 39].
5.3
Face wrinkling
Face wrinkling is a failure mode observed in sandwich elements under compression or
bending, presenting itself as a localized short-wavelength buckling of the compression face
[39, 40]. The failure can occur in two ways: a) an unstable wrinkle causes an indentation
in the core layer if the compressive strength of the core is lower than the tensile strength
of the core and the adhesive joint, or b) the wrinkle causes a tensile fracture if the tensile
strength of the core or the adhesive joint is lower than the compressive strength of the
core. Regardless of the scenario, the wrinkling failure stress remains constant. Poor-
quality adhesive is likely to reduce the wrinkling stress criteria [21].
As discussed in Section 5.2, core shear failure can trigger face wrinkling, which is
more common for short-span elements. However, for long-span elements wrinkling can
occur even before any core failure [39]. Since sandwich structures may have little to no
post-wrinkling load bearing capacity, failure due to wrinkling is typically catastrophic
[41].
14
A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels
94
==PAGE:113==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
5.4
General buckling
While general buckling in a sandwich structure may not always lead to a catastrophic
failure, given that the faces have not been crushed or yielded, a buckled structure typically
has lost its capability in fulfilling its purpose. For this reason, buckling load may be used
to determine the load bearing capacity of a system. If loading post-buckling persists, the
deformation will increase leading to failure. The final failure modes include a) face on the
compressive side failing in compression, b) wrinkling of the compressive face or c) core
shear failure due to excessive deformation, resulting in accumulation of transverse forces.
The latter visually resembles shear crimping described in Section 5.5 [21].
5.5
Shear crimping (buckling)
Shear crimping (buckling), as introduced in Section 5.4, is likely to occur during large
out-of-plane deformations in a post buckled state, where the accumulation of transverse
forces due to localized deformations can take place. Once the shear stress in a buckled
element exceeds the critical threshold, a sudden shear buckling failure occurs [21].
5.6
Face dimpling
Face dimpling, also known as monocell buckling, is a characteristic instability phenomenon
observed in sandwich structures featuring honeycomb or corrugated cores. It is induced
by compressive loads and involves the instability of the facing material covering a single
cell, taking the appearance of concave indentations or dimples on the surface of the facing
material [2, 21].
5.7
Local indentation
Local indentation is a failure mode observed under highly localized external loads, like
point or line loads. This failure mode involves local yielding and deformation of the core
material beneath the heavily loaded area, followed by a significant local deformation of the
loaded facing into the core, ultimately leading to either face crushing or core shear failure.
Local indentation failure mode can be effectively avoided by spreading the concentrated
loads over a sufficiently large area [21, 38, 39].
5.8
Debonding
Debonding occurs when the adhesive joint between the face and the core fails, losing its
bond, This can result from issues such as the use of unsuitable adhesive, errors in the
application process during manufacturing or external factors such as impact damage or
overloading. The shear stress in the bond line is nearly as high as in the middle of the
core. Therefore, if the adhesive bond strength is less than the coreтАЩs, it will fail before the
core does. Debonding results in reduced stiffness, bearing capacity and the possibility of
other failure modes [21, 39].
15
A Literature Review: Sandwich Panels
95
==PAGE:114==
Literature review
Designing for full composite action
6
Conclusions
When appropriately designed, sandwich structures present significant potential for mate-
rial efficiency. Main conclusions drawn from the literature study include:
1. Full composite action is essential for material efficiency, load bearing capacity and
structural behavior prediction of a sandwich structure. When achieved, all com-
ponents function as a single unit resulting with more efficient load transfer and
improved structural performance.
2. To maximize structural efficiency, it is important to understand the potential failure
modes associated with the specific application and to design accordingly.
3. Shear connectors are crucial in achieving full composite action and preventing pre-
mature failure, particularly in sandwich elements utilizing low-density foam cores.
4. Optimization of the thickness ratio between the layers is essential for high material
efficiency. A higher core thickness to face ratio generally reduces shear flow, resulting
in reduced degree of composite action.
Recommendations for designing for full composite action:
1. Prioritize the use of extensively researched shear connector types known for their
high strength and stiffness.
These connectors should ensure high shear transfer
capacity and maintain their structural integrity over extended periods of loading,
providing the baseline for high degree or full composite action.
2. When selecting shear connector type, asses the particular application and consider
the trade-offs, i.e. selecting FRP connectors only if thermal bridging is of a concern,
given their considerably lower stiffness compared to a steel truss connector.
3. For a sandwich element with a defined geometry, material properties, loading and
boundary conditions, perform analysis using established mathematical or finite ele-
ment models to determine appropriate shear connector arrangement and spacing.
4. When targeting improved material efficiency, refrain from an excessive increase in
core thickness, thus avoiding the diminishing effect of reduced composite action.
5. Consider utilizing high or ultra-high strength materials for sandwich faces coupled
with a reduced thickness ratio, increasing the degree of composite action.
6. Tailor the layer composition according to the use case by doing failure mapping
for the most critical failure modes. Such optimization approach promotes material
efficiency and composite action through premature failure prevention.
7. Ensure that the selected core material and adhesive between the layers has sufficient
mechanical properties to withstand both in-plane and out-of-plane shear stresses for
a given section.
16

There are four sections along the longitudinal profile of the element that must be considered:
A) Transverse wall - WALL
B) Section right before the wall - END
C) Mid point - MID
D) Mortar joint - JOINT

Above this bottom layer is a layer 3. It consists of the curved parts of the grooves at
the sides of the blocks + the area of width wG4 next to the blocks and hight as the
hight of the curved parts.  
Layer 3  is the curved concrete grooves between blocks
Properties of a half groove between two block are derived in an Appendix to the
calculations, and is entered from that. The static moment S and hight e are taken
about the bottom of the slab.
The curved part of a half groove between two blocks is called a "bay".

Ultimate resistance of the cross-section
The following procedure is applied:
1) Maximum possible normal force NLmax that can act on the cross-section is found.
2) Then the moment of that MNLmax taken about the depth H/2 is calculated.

knowing NL.

3. Sandwich section neutral axis
The following assumptions are in place for this calculation:
1) The centroid of the Post-Tensioning (PT) cable follows the arch shape function;
2) The cassette panels are assumed to be curved and follow the curvature of the PT cable;

Pervious concrete backfill:
Due to the geometry of the cassette element and the polygonal shape of the PC-arch, the following assumptions are made for a
conservative approximation for the backfill height distribution:
1) The centroid of the Post-Tensioning (PT) cable follows the arch shape function;
2) The cassette panels are assumed to be curved and follow the curvature of the PT cable;
3) The concrete volume occupied by cassette transverse walls is neglected, assuming uniform cross-section throughout;
Total pervious concrete cross-section:
тЙФ
ASB.pc.tot ((x))
+

ASB.pc.c ((x))

тЙФ
gbf.k ((x))
тЛЕ
тЛЕ
╧Бpc g ASB.pc.tot ((x))
Pervious concrete backfill (uneven distribution):
This load case assumes that during backfill pouring, incremental steps of 0.25 m in backfill height are taken 
between the sides. The most unfavorable load distribution is considered at the last step before reaching crown 
height, where one side is fully backfilled, and the other side has 0.25 m less in fill height.

12. Cassette slab and transverse wall reinforcement
Cassette slab
In a cassette cross-section, the middle slab can be viewed as a simply 
supported beam that must be able to carry the self-weight of the backfill 
material during pouring operations.
